Suretyship for Future Maintenance: Abu Yusuf's Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala for future maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suretyship for maintenance before it is fixed and explain why Abu Yusuf's istihsan prevails as the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Suretyship for future maintenance is invalid in general, but Abu Yusuf's istihsan makes it valid for one month—and the fatwa follows him.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical problem: a wife whose husband is about to travel wants a guarantor for her future maintenance. The general rule requires an existing debt for suretyship, but Abu Yusuf invokes istihsan (juristic preference) to allow it for one month. The fatwa (alayhi al-fatwa) adopts this lenient view. Understanding this shows how Hanafi law balances strict rules with necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The author states that suretyship for maintenance before it is fixed (by court or agreement) is invalid. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
The underlying principle: kafala requires an existing debt.
2
Locate Abu Yusuf's exception
Abu Yusuf uses istihsan to allow it for one month when the husband intends to travel. The text says: 'استحسن ذلك وآخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة شهراً وعليه الفتوى'.
Istihsan is justified because maintenance will become obligatory soon.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the majority view, the wife cannot secure a guarantor until the debt is fixed. Under Abu Yusuf, she can do so immediately, protecting her interests.
This practical difference is why the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
If a husband is about to travel and the wife wants a guarantor for three months, the mufta bihi view (Abu Yusuf) would allow only one month unless a specific amount per month is stated and the guarantee is for all months (some reports say forever).
The default is one month; extension requires explicit agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أن الكفالة بالنفقة قبل الفرض أو التراضي على معين لا تصح وبعد كما تصح في الذخيرة: ولو أن المرأة قالت للقاضي إن زوجي يريد أن يغيب وأرادت أن تأخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة فإنه ليس لها ذلك لأن النفقة لم تجب. وقال أبو يوسف : استحسن ذلك وآخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة شهراً وعليه الفتوى لأن النفقة إن لم تجب للحال تجب بعده فتصير كأنه كفل بما ذاب لها على الزوج فيجبر استحساناً رفقاً بالناس؛ كذا في الواقعات. زاد في الذخيرة أنه لا فرق في هذا الحكم بين أن تكون النفقة مفروضة أو لا. وفي الذخيرة أيضاً: ولو اختلفا فيما مضى من المدة من وقت القضاء أو من وقت الصلح فالقول قول الزوج والبينة بينة المرأة لأنها تدعي زيادة دين والزوج ينكر فالقول قوله مع يمينه، وإذا ادعى الزوج الإنفاق وأنكرت المرأة فالقول قولها مع اليمين كما في سائر الديون اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : امرأة أقامت على رجل بينة بالنكاح فلا نفقة لها في مدة المسألة عن الشهود، ولو أراد القاضي أن يفرض لها النفقة لما رأى من المصلحة ينبغي أن يقول لها إن كنت امرأته فقد فرضت ذلك عليه في كل شهر كذا وكذا ويشهد على ذلك، فإذا مضى شهر وقد استدانت وعدلت البيئة آخذته بنفقتها منذ فرض لها اهـ وهو يدل على ما قلنا من أن الفرض من القاضي يصيرها ديناً فلا تسقط بالمضي، وإن فرض القاضي النفقة قضاء لا يقال إنه ليس بقضاء لعدم الدعوى لأنا نقول : طلبها التقدير دعوى ومسألة الإبراء تدل على أن الفرض في الشهر الأول تنجز وفيما بعده مضاف فتنجز بدخول الشهر وهكذا فلا الرجوع عنه لما يصح الخانية من الصلح ولو صالحت المرأة زوجها عن نفقة كل شهر على دراهم ثم قال الزوج : قوله : (زاد في الذخيرة أنه لا فرق (الخ) قال الرملي : نقل في التتارخانية عن الذخيرة في النفقات بقوله : وفي الذخيرة في كتاب الأقضية في رجل ضمن لامرأته النفقة والمهر فإن ضمان النفقة باطل إلا أن يسمى لكل شهر شيئاً ومعناه أن الزوج مع المرأة يصطلحان على شيء مقدر لنفقة كل شهر ثم يضمنه رجل فحينئذ يجوز الضمان ولكن لا يلزمه الضمان أكثر من شهر ا هـ. فجوازها مع عدم الفرض في مسألة مريد الغيبة استحسان تأمل وتقدم أنه لو كفل بالنفقة كل شهر عشرة دراهم لزمه شهر، وعند أبي يوسف يقع على الأبد وعليه الفتوى وذكر في الخلاصة أن الأب لا
The author indicated that suretyship for maintenance before a court order or mutual agreement on a specific amount is invalid, and after it is valid as in al-Dhakhira: If a woman said to the judge, 'My husband wants to travel and I want to take a guarantor from him for maintenance,' she does not have that because maintenance has not become obligatory. Abu Yusuf said: I consider it good (istihsan) and take a guarantor from him for a month's maintenance, and the fatwa is on that, because maintenance, if not obligatory immediately, becomes obligatory afterwards, so it is as if he guaranteed what is due to her from the husband, enforced by istihsan for the benefit of people, as in al-Waqi'at. He added in al-Dhakhira that there is no difference in this ruling between maintenance being fixed or not. Also in al-Dhakhira: If they differ regarding the period that has passed from the time of the court order or the time of the settlement, the word is the husband's and the burden of proof is on the woman because she claims an increase of debt and the husband denies, so his word with his oath. If the husband claims he paid and the woman denies, her word with her oath as in other debts. In al-Zahiriya: A woman establishes proof of marriage against a man, she has no maintenance for the period of questioning the witnesses. If the judge wants to fix maintenance for her due to perceived benefit, he should say: 'If you are his wife, I have fixed that upon him such-and-such each month,' and testify to that. When a month passes and she has borrowed and the witnesses are upright, she takes her maintenance from the time he fixed for her. This indicates what we said: that the judge's fixing makes it a debt, so it does not drop by lapse of time. And if the judge fixes maintenance as a judgment, it is not said that it is not a judgment due to lack of claim, for we say: her request for determination is a claim. The issue of renunciation indicates that the fixing for the first month is realized and for subsequent months it is attached, so it becomes realized by the entry of the month, and so on. So there is no turning back from what is valid in al-Khaniyya from settlement. If the woman settles with her husband for the maintenance of each month for a sum, then... His saying: (He added in al-Dhakhira that there is no difference...) Al-Ramli said: It is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Dhakhira regarding maintenance: In al-Dhakhira in the book of Judgments, concerning a man who guaranteed for a woman her maintenance and dowry: the guarantee of maintenance is invalid unless he specifies for each month something. Its meaning: the husband and wife agree on a fixed amount for the maintenance of each month, then a man guarantees it; then the guarantee is valid, but he is not liable for more than one month. Thus its permissibility without a court order in the case of one intending to travel is istihsan. Consider: It was mentioned earlier that if he guaranteed maintenance of ten dirhams each month, he is liable for one month. According to Abu Yusuf, it applies forever, and the fatwa is on that. It is mentioned in al-Khulasa that the father does not...
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage discusses suretyship (kafala) for maintenance (nafaqa) before it becomes a fixed debt. The general rule is that kafala for an obligation that does not yet exist is invalid (batil). However, Abu Yusuf used istihsan (juristic preference) to allow a woman whose husband is about to travel to take a guarantor for a month's maintenance, because the obligation will arise soon. This is a concession for the benefit of people (rifqan bi al-nas). The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. The ruling also addresses disputes: if the couple argues about past payments, the husband's word with his oath is accepted if he denies the debt, but the wife's word is accepted if she denies payment. The text also nuances that if the husband and wife agree on a specific monthly amount, a third party can guarantee that, but only for one month by default, though Abu Yusuf holds it applies forever. The passage ends incompletely but establishes the istihsan-based permissibility.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; deviation from a strict analogy due to equity or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), when is suretyship (kafala) for maintenance valid before a judge's order?
A) Never, because maintenance is not yet a debt.
B) Only if the wife agrees to it.
C) Valid for one month if the husband is about to travel, based on istihsan.
D) Valid indefinitely if the amount is specified.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for future maintenance is normally invalid, but Abu Yusuf's istihsan makes it valid for one month when needed, and the fatwa follows this concession.

Procedural Aspects: Burden of Proof and Debt Fixation in Maintenance
Procedural rules on proof and debt fixation in maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on burden of proof in maintenance disputes and explain how a judge's fixing (farḍ) converts future maintenance into a debt.

Core Idea
In maintenance disputes, the husband's word is taken for past payments, but the wife's word is taken for non-payment; the judge's fixing makes maintenance a debt that does not lapse.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues with procedural rules for maintenance disputes. It explains who has the burden of proof when the couple argues about past payments, and how a judge's order (farḍ) turns maintenance into a fixed debt that continues until paid. It also addresses the scenario of a woman proving marriage but lacking witnesses for the duration, and the judge's role in setting a prospective amount.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of dispute
If the couple argues about whether maintenance was paid in the past, the husband's word with his oath is accepted if he denies the debt. If the husband claims he paid but the wife denies, the wife's word with her oath is accepted.
This follows general principles: the claimant bears the burden, but for payment, the one who denies receipt (wife) is believed.
2
Understand the effect of the judge's fixing (farḍ)
When the judge fixes maintenance (even without a formal claim, e.g., when the wife says 'if I am his wife'), that fixing makes maintenance a debt from that moment. It does not drop by lapse of time.
The text emphasizes that the judge's statement 'if you are his wife, I have fixed...' creates an enforceable debt.
3
Apply the rule of progressive liability
The fixing for the first month is immediate; for subsequent months, it is contingent on each month's entry. So the liability becomes due month by month.
This prevents the entire future maintenance from being due at once.
4
Note the settlement (ṣulḥ) scenario
If the wife settles with her husband for a fixed sum per month and a third party guarantees that, the guarantee is valid only for one month unless otherwise specified.
This connects back to the first lesson's suretyship rules.

The author indicated that suretyship for maintenance before a court order or mutual agreement on a specific amount is invalid, and after it is valid as in al-Dhakhira: If a woman said to the judge, 'My husband wants to travel and I want to take a guarantor from him for maintenance,' she does not have that because maintenance has not become obligatory. Abu Yusuf said: I consider it good (istihsan) and take a guarantor from him for a month's maintenance, and the fatwa is on that, because maintenance, if not obligatory immediately, becomes obligatory afterwards, so it is as if he guaranteed what is due to her from the husband, enforced by istihsan for the benefit of people, as in al-Waqi'at. He added in al-Dhakhira that there is no difference in this ruling between maintenance being fixed or not. Also in al-Dhakhira: If they differ regarding the period that has passed from the time of the court order or the time of the settlement, the word is the husband's and the burden of proof is on the woman because she claims an increase of debt and the husband denies, so his word with his oath. If the husband claims he paid and the woman denies, her word with her oath as in other debts. In al-Zahiriya: A woman establishes proof of marriage against a man, she has no maintenance for the period of questioning the witnesses. If the judge wants to fix maintenance for her due to perceived benefit, he should say: 'If you are his wife, I have fixed that upon him such-and-such each month,' and testify to that. When a month passes and she has borrowed and the witnesses are upright, she takes her maintenance from the time he fixed for her. This indicates what we said: that the judge's fixing makes it a debt, so it does not drop by lapse of time. And if the judge fixes maintenance as a judgment, it is not said that it is not a judgment due to lack of claim, for we say: her request for determination is a claim. The issue of renunciation indicates that the fixing for the first month is realized and for subsequent months it is attached, so it becomes realized by the entry of the month, and so on. So there is no turning back from what is valid in al-Khaniyya from settlement. If the woman settles with her husband for the maintenance of each month for a sum, then... His saying: (He added in al-Dhakhira that there is no difference...) Al-Ramli said: It is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Dhakhira regarding maintenance: In al-Dhakhira in the book of Judgments, concerning a man who guaranteed for a woman her maintenance and dowry: the guarantee of maintenance is invalid unless he specifies for each month something. Its meaning: the husband and wife agree on a fixed amount for the maintenance of each month, then a man guarantees it; then the guarantee is valid, but he is not liable for more than one month. Thus its permissibility without a court order in the case of one intending to travel is istihsan. Consider: It was mentioned earlier that if he guaranteed maintenance of ten dirhams each month, he is liable for one month. According to Abu Yusuf, it applies forever, and the fatwa is on that. It is mentioned in al-Khulasa that the father does not...

Commentary
This passage discusses suretyship (kafala) for maintenance (nafaqa) before it becomes a fixed debt. The general rule is that kafala for an obligation that does not yet exist is invalid (batil). However, Abu Yusuf used istihsan (juristic preference) to allow a woman whose husband is about to travel to take a guarantor for a month's maintenance, because the obligation will arise soon. This is a concession for the benefit of people (rifqan bi al-nas). The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. The ruling also addresses disputes: if the couple argues about past payments, the husband's word with his oath is accepted if he denies the debt, but the wife's word is accepted if she denies payment. The text also nuances that if the husband and wife agree on a specific monthly amount, a third party can guarantee that, but only for one month by default, though Abu Yusuf holds it applies forever. The passage ends incompletely but establishes the istihsan-based permissibility.

Key Terms
فرض القاضي (farḍ al-qāḍī): The judge's fixing of an amount of maintenance; it creates an enforceable debt.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Proof; evidence that establishes a claim in court.

Suretyship for Maintenance Before Fixing: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah before fixing maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on suretyship for maintenance before it is fixed and state the mufta bihi position with its justification.

Core Idea
Though Abu Hanifa rejects suretyship for unfixed maintenance, Abu Yusuf's istihsan permits it for one month, and this is the fatwa.

This lesson explores a practical point of Hanafi family law: can a wife demand a guarantor for her maintenance before the amount is fixed by a judge or mutual agreement? The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq states it is invalid, but a decisive intra-madhhab ruling from Abu Yusuf, based on istihsan (juristic preference), allows it for one month. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish strict analogy from equitable reasoning in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is kafalah (suretyship) for nafaqah (maintenance) before it is fixed by a judge or mutual agreement. The dispute is about its legal validity.
The base text (Kanz) says it is not valid; Abu Yusuf says it is valid by istihsan.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that kafalah before fixing is invalid because maintenance is not yet a present debt. Guaranteeing a non-existent debt is invalid by analogy to kafalah for future debts.
This is strict qiyas: the asl is that kafalah requires an existing obligation.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his istihsan justification
Abu Yusuf uses istihsan: though maintenance is not yet due at the present moment, it will become obligatory very soon (it is an imminent debt). Treating it as a present debt for the purpose of guarantee is a kindness (rifq) to the wife. He limits the guarantee to one month.
The text says: 'استحسن ذلك وآخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة شهراً'.
4
Trace the tarjih and the fatwa
The text explicitly states 'وعليه الفتوى' (and upon it is the fatwa). This means the relied-upon position for issuing fatwas is Abu Yusuf's view.
Tarjih authority: implicit from the phrase, indicating the later Hanafi consensus.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and the limitation
The practical consequence: a wife who fears her husband will abscond can obtain a guarantor for one month of maintenance, even without a court order or mutual agreement. This facilitation applies whether the maintenance is fixed or not, as added by al-Dhakhira.
The fatwa is limited to one month; beyond that, the original ruling may apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى أن الكفالة بالنفقة قبل الفرض أو التراضي على معين لا تصح وبعد كما تصح في الذخيرة: ولو أن المرأة قالت للقاضي إن زوجي يريد أن يغيب وأرادت أن تأخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة فإنه ليس لها ذلك لأن النفقة لم تجب. وقال أبو يوسف : استحسن ذلك وآخذ منه كفيلاً بالنفقة شهراً وعليه الفتوى لأن النفقة إن لم تجب للحال تجب بعده فتصير كأنه كفل بما ذاب لها على الزوج فيجبر استحساناً رفقاً بالناس؛ كذا في الواقعات. زاد في الذخيرة أنه لا فرق في هذا الحكم بين أن تكون النفقة مفروضة أو لا.
And the author pointed out that suretyship for maintenance before it is fixed by a judge or before mutual agreement on a specific amount is not valid, and after it is fixed it is valid as stated in al-Dhakhira. If a woman says to the judge: 'My husband wants to go away and I want to take a guarantor from him for maintenance', then she does not have that right because maintenance has not become obligatory. Abu Yusuf said: I consider this istihsan, and I take a guarantor from him for maintenance for one month, and the fatwa is upon this, because even if maintenance is not obligatory at the present moment, it will become obligatory afterwards, so it is as if he has guaranteed something that is due to her from the husband, so he is compelled by istihsan as a kindness to people. Thus in al-Waqi'at. Al-Dhakhira added that there is no difference in this ruling between whether the maintenance is fixed or not.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of suretyship (kafalah) for maintenance before it becomes a fixed obligation. The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) indicates that kafalah before the judge's decree (qada) or mutual agreement on a specific amount is invalid; it is valid only after such fixing. However, Abu Yusuf uses the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to allow it for one month, arguing that although maintenance is not yet a present debt, it will become obligatory soon, so treating it as a present debt for the purpose of guarantee is a kindness (rifq) to people. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's view. The addition from al-Dhakhira clarifies that this ruling applies whether the maintenance has been fixed by a judge or not, indicating that the relevant consideration is the imminent obligation, not formal fixation.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship or guarantee; a contract where a person becomes a guarantor for an obligation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling.
فرض (fard): In this context, the fixing or decree of the judge regarding the amount of maintenance; also can mean obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Forever
B) One month
C) One year
D) Not allowed at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa on suretyship for unfixed maintenance follows Abu Yusuf's istihsan, permitting a one-month guarantee out of kindness to wives.

Burden of Proof When Spouses Disagree on Past Maintenance
Evidentiary rules for disputes about past maintenance payments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidentiary rule for disputes about past maintenance and apply it to the two scenarios where the wife claims non-payment and where the husband claims payment.

Core Idea
The denier's word is accepted with an oath, whether husband or wife.

When spouses disagree about past maintenance payments, the Hanafi school follows the general principle of debt disputes: the party who denies an obligation has their word taken with an oath. This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two specific scenarios and shows that the rule is symmetric, not biased toward the husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (a) the wife claims the husband did not pay (she claims a debt), (b) the husband claims he paid (he claims discharge). Note that each scenario flips who is the claimant and who is the denier.
2
Apply the general rule: al-qawl qawlahu
Recall that in financial claims, the defendant's word (with oath) is accepted. The claimant must provide evidence (bayyinah). Identify who is the defendant in each scenario.
The rule is based on the principle that the denial of a debt is more credible than the claim because the claimant may be mistaken or lying.
3
Map the ruling to each scenario
Scenario 1: wife claims non-payment → she is claiming a debt, husband denies → husband's word with oath; wife must provide evidence. Scenario 2: husband claims payment → he claims discharge (extinguishing a debt), wife denies receipt → she denies the debt → wife's word with oath; husband must provide evidence.
The text explicitly says 'as in other debts' to emphasize this symmetry.
4
Note the time reference
The period in question is from the time of the judge's decree (qada) or reconciliation (sulh). This frames the subject of dispute and may affect the starting point of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة أيضاً: ولو اختلفا فيما مضى من المدة من وقت القضاء أو من وقت الصلح فالقول قول الزوج والبينة بينة المرأة لأنها تدعي زيادة دين والزوج ينكر فالقول قوله مع يمينه، وإذا ادعى الزوج الإنفاق وأنكرت المرأة فالقول قولها مع اليمين كما في سائر الديون اهـ.
And in al-Dhakhira also: If they differ about the past period from the time of the judge's decree or from the time of reconciliation, the husband's word is taken and the burden of proof is on the wife because she claims an increase of debt and the husband denies, so his word is taken with his oath. And if the husband claims that he spent (paid maintenance) and the woman denies, then her word is taken with her oath as in other debts.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage provides evidentiary rules for when spouses disagree about maintenance payments that are allegedly due from the past. The rule follows general principles of debt disputes: the party denying a debt has their word accepted with an oath, while the party claiming additional debt must bring evidence (bayyinah). If the wife claims that the husband never paid maintenance (i.e., that he owes her), she is claiming a debt, so the husband's word is accepted with his oath that he paid or that no debt is due. Conversely, if the husband claims he paid and the wife denies receiving, the wife is denying the debt, so her word is accepted with her oath. This is consistent with the general rules for all debts, as the text says 'as in other debts.' The time reference is from when the judge fixed maintenance (qada) or from when they reconciled (sulh) on an amount.

Key Terms
القول قوله (al-qawl qawlahu): The statement of the party (usually the defendant) is taken as true with an oath; this is the default in debt disputes.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually consisting of two male witnesses or one male and two females in financial matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The wife's word
C) Both must provide two male witnesses
D) The judge decides based on the greater likelihood

Key Takeaway
In disputes over past maintenance, the denier's word is accepted with an oath, following the general principle for all debts.

Conditional Fixing of Maintenance During Witness Examination
Ruling on judge's conditional maintenance decree during witness examination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance during witness examination and explain the judge's conditional fixing procedure based on maslaha.

Core Idea
Maintenance is not due until marriage is proven, but a judge may provisionally fix it with a conditional decree to prevent hardship.

When a woman claims marriage but proof is pending due to witness examination, the default rule is no maintenance for her. However, the Hanafi school allows a judge, acting on public benefit (maslaha), to conditionally fix maintenance. This lesson explores the procedure and legal effect of such a decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Recognize that during the period of questioning witnesses ('mas'alah 'an al-shuhud'), the woman has no right to maintenance from the man.
This is because the marriage is not yet proven.
2
Recognize the judge's discretionary power
The judge may, based on maslaha (public benefit), provisionally fix maintenance for the woman, even though the marriage is not confirmed.
Maslaha here is to prevent the woman from suffering hardship while waiting for the marriage to be proven.
3
Analyze the conditional wording
The judge says, 'If you are his wife, I have fixed this upon him.' This conditional phrasing ensures the decree is not a final ruling but contingent on the marriage being proven.
The judge also takes witnesses to this conditional fixing.
4
Trace the legal effect after proof
If after a month the witnesses are found upright and marriage is proven, and the woman has borrowed to support herself, the husband becomes obligated to pay maintenance from the date of the conditional fixing.
This shows that the conditional fixing creates a retroactive obligation once the marriage is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : امرأة أقامت على رجل بينة بالنكاح فلا نفقة لها في مدة المسألة عن الشهود، ولو أراد القاضي أن يفرض لها النفقة لما رأى من المصلحة ينبغي أن يقول لها إن كنت امرأته فقد فرضت ذلك عليه في كل شهر كذا وكذا ويشهد على ذلك، فإذا مضى شهر وقد استدانت وعدلت البيئة آخذته بنفقتها منذ فرض لها اهـ
In al-Zahiriyya: A woman presents evidence of marriage against a man; she has no maintenance during the period of questioning the witnesses. If the judge wants to fix maintenance for her due to what he sees of benefit, he should say to her: 'If you are his wife, I have fixed this upon him every month, such and such,' and he takes witnesses to that. Then when a month has passed and she has borrowed and the witnesses are upright, he obligates him with her maintenance from when it was fixed for her.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario where a woman brings a marriage claim with witnesses, but the marriage is not yet proven because the witnesses are being examined (the period of 'mas'alah 'an al-shuhud'). During this period, she is not entitled to maintenance. However, the judge may, as a matter of maslaha (public benefit), provisionally fix maintenance for her. He does so conditionally, using the phrase 'If you are his wife, I have fixed...' to avoid issuing a final ruling without certainty. He also takes witnesses to this conditional decree. If after a month the marriage is established (the witnesses are found upright and the judge rules in her favor), and she has incurred debt (istadanat) to support herself, the husband is obligated to pay her maintenance from the date of that conditional fixing. This demonstrates that the judge's provisional fixing creates a debt effective from that date, provided the marriage is eventually proven.

Key Terms
مسألة عن الشهود (mas'alah 'an al-shuhud): The period during which the judge investigates and examines the credibility of the witnesses presented by the woman in a marriage claim.
مصلحة (maslaha): Benefit or public interest; here, a basis for the judge to act provisionally to avoid hardship for the woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is entitled to full maintenance
B) She has no maintenance
C) The judge must fix maintenance immediately
D) The husband must provide support if he is rich

Key Takeaway
A judge may provisionally fix maintenance with a conditional decree during witness examination, and if marriage is later proven, the husband owes from that date.

Maintenance Settlement: Taqdir vs Mu'awada
Rulings on maintenance settlement and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a maintenance settlement that is a taqdir (determination) and one that is a mu'awada (exchange), and apply the rules of adjustment for each.

Core Idea
A settlement on maintenance is a taqdir (adjustable) if the judge could have fixed that amount; otherwise, it is a mu'awada (binding).

In Hanafi fiqh, when a judge fixes maintenance, it becomes a compulsory debt. However, spouses may also settle on an amount. This lesson explores when such a settlement is final and when it can be adjusted, based on the crucial distinction between taqdir and mu'awada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the judge's decree makes maintenance a compulsory debt (dayn), and that the husband may refuse excess beyond his capacity.
The text states: 'We have previously mentioned that if he fixes upon him more than his capacity, he has the right to refuse the increase.'
2
Classify the type of settlement
Determine whether the subject of the settlement is something the judge could normally order as maintenance (like cash) or something unusual (like a garment or a slave). If it is the former, the settlement is a taqdir; if the latter and it occurs after a judicial decree or mutual agreement, it is a mu'awada.
The text says: '...if the settlement is for something that the judge is not allowed to fix as maintenance at all, like a garment or a slave, then it is considered...'
3
Trace the consequence
For a taqdir, increase or decrease is permissible if circumstances change. For a mu'awada, the amount is fixed and cannot be altered by either party.
The text explicitly states the benefits: 'the benefit of considering it a determination is that increase and decrease are permissible; the benefit of considering it an exchange is that increase or decrease upon that amount is not permissible.'
4
Handle the husband's claim of inability
If the husband voluntarily agreed to a sum, his later claim of inability is not accepted without independent verification by the judge through inquiry.
The text says: '...if the man says I cannot bear it, he is not believed in that, because he undertook it voluntarily... unless the judge inquires about his condition by asking people...'

وقد علم من عطف المصنف الرضا على القضاء أن فرض القاضي بطريق الجبر وقدمنا أنه إذا فرض عليه أكثر من حاله فإن له أن يمتنع عن الزيادة، وكذا إذا اصطلحا على أزيد من نفقة المثل لما في الظهيرية : وإذا صالح الرجل امرأته عن نفقة كل شهر على مائة درهم والزوج محتاج لم يلزمه إلا نفقة مثلها، وإذا صالحها على دائق كل شهر جاز ولها أن تنقض إن لم يكفها اهـ . وفي الذخيرة : وإذا صالحت المرأة زوجها من نفقتها على ثلاثة دراهم كل شهر فهو جائز وكان ذلك تقديراً لنفقتها . والأصل أن الصلح بينهما متى حصل بشيء يجوز للقاضي أن يفرضه في نفقتها بحال فالصلح بينهما تقدير للنفقة ولا تعتبر معاوضة، سواء كان هذا الصلح قبل فرض القاضي أو التراضي على شيء أو كان بعد أحدهما. وإذا وقع الصلح على شيء لا يجوز للقاضي أن يفرضه على الزوج في نفقتها بحال كالثوب والعبد ينظر إن كان الصلح بينهما قبل قضاء القاضي لها بالنفقة وقبل تراضيهما على شيء لكل شهر يعتبر الصلح منهما تقديراً وبعد أحدهما يعتبر معاوضة وفائدة اعتبار التقدير أن تجوز الزيادة عليه والنقصان عنه وفائدة اعتبار المعاوضة أن لا تجوز الزيادة على ذلك ولا النقصان فإذا صالحها على دراهم كل شهر ثم قالت لا تكفيني زيدت ولو قال الرجل لا أطيقه فإنه لا يصدق في ذلك فإنه التزمه باختياره، وذلك دليل على كونه قادراً على أداء ما التزم فيلزمه ذلك إلا أن يتعرف القاضي عن حاله بالسؤال من الناس فإذا أخبروه أنه لا يطيق ذلك نقص عنه وأوجب على قدر طاقته، فإن لم يمض شيء من الشهر حتى صالحها جميع من هذه يطالب بمهر زوجة ابنه ونفقتها إلا أن يضمن وأطلق فظاهره جواز الضمان مطلقاً إلا أن يحمل على المقيد وحمله عليه متعين توفيقاً بين كلامهم ا هـ . . أقول : قد يقال يشترط ذلك في مسألة مريد السفر

We have previously mentioned that if he fixes upon him more than his capacity, he has the right to refuse the increase. Likewise, if they settle on an amount more than the proper maintenance (nafaqa al-mithl), based on what is in al-Zahiriyya: If a man settles with his wife for one hundred dirhams per month for maintenance and the husband is in need, only the proper maintenance is binding upon him. If he settles with her for a daniq per month, it is valid, and she has the right to revoke if it does not suffice her. And in al-Dhakhira: If a woman settles with her husband for three dirhams per month for her maintenance, it is valid and that is a determination (taqdir) of her maintenance. The principle is that whenever a settlement between them occurs for something that the judge is allowed to fix as maintenance in her case, the settlement is a determination (taqdir) of maintenance, not considered an exchange (mu'awada), whether this settlement is before the judge's decree or before mutual agreement on something, or after either. If the settlement is for something that the judge is not allowed to fix as maintenance at all, like a garment or a slave, then it is considered: if the settlement was before the judge's decree for her maintenance and before their mutual agreement on something for each month, the settlement is considered a determination; after either, it is considered an exchange. The benefit of considering it a determination is that increase and decrease are permissible; the benefit of considering it an exchange is that increase or decrease upon that amount is not permissible. So if he settles with her for dirhams each month, then she says 'it does not suffice me', it is increased; and if the man says 'I cannot bear it', he is not believed in that, because he undertook it voluntarily, and that is evidence that he is able to pay what he undertook, so it is binding upon him, unless the judge inquires about his condition by asking people, and if they inform him that he cannot bear it, he reduces it and obliges according to his capacity. If nothing of the month has passed until she settled with him... all of this... he is sued for the mahr of his son's wife and her maintenance unless he guarantees... and he spoke generally, so its apparent meaning is the validity of the guarantee absolutely, unless it is restricted and its restriction is necessary for reconciliation between their statements. I say: It may be said that this condition is required in the case of one intending travel.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage consolidates several rulings about the nature of a judge's decree (qada) regarding maintenance and the effect of settlement (sulh) between spouses. The key principle established is that when the judge fixes maintenance, it becomes a compulsory debt (dayn) that does not fall due simply with the passage of time; it remains a debt until paid or otherwise discharged. The husband may refuse to pay if the amount fixed exceeds his capacity. Settlement is a distinct mechanism: if the spouses agree on an amount that the judge would normally be allowed to fix (like cash in a reasonable range), the settlement is considered a 'taqdir' (determination) of maintenance, not an 'mu'awada' (exchange). This means it sets a benchmark, and either party can later request increase or decrease based on changed circumstances. If they settle on something the judge would never order (like a garment or slave), then the timing matters: if before any judicial decree or mutual agreement, it is taqdir; after that, it is mu'awada, binding and not subject to adjustment. The passage also covers the husband's inability to pay: his claim of inability after voluntarily agreeing to an amount is not accepted unless the judge independently verifies his situation from reliable sources. The end of the passage discusses a problematic scenario about a guarantor for the mahr of the son's wife and her maintenance, with a note that it may require restriction.

Key Terms
تقدير (taqdir): Determination or apportionment; a settlement that sets the amount of maintenance, allowing future adjustment.
معاوضة (mu'awada): Exchange or commutative contract; here it refers to a settlement that is considered a final exchange, not subject to adjustment.
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise between parties; in this context, an agreement on the amount of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband settles with his wife on a specific amount of dirhams per month for maintenance, and later she claims it does not suffice, what happens according to the text?
A) She cannot increase it because the settlement is final.
B) It is increased if she proves insufficiency.
C) The settlement is void.
D) The husband can unilaterally decrease it.

Key Takeaway
A settlement on maintenance is either a taqdir (determination, adjustable) or a mu'awada (exchange, binding), depending on whether the judge could have ordered such an amount.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Taqdir vs Mu'awadah in Spousal Maintenance Compromises
Explains when a spousal compromise is estimation vs exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish taqdir from mu'awadah in spousal maintenance compromises and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether a compromise can be adjusted.

Core Idea
A spousal maintenance compromise is an adjustable estimate if the judge could have ordered it; otherwise it is a binding exchange.

When spouses agree on maintenance, the nature of that agreement determines if later adjustments are possible. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi distinction: if the agreed item is something a judge could impose (like money), it is an estimate (taqdir), changeable by need; if it's a specific object (like a garment), it is an exchange (mu'awadah), binding both parties. Understanding this distinction prevents unfair binding and allows for equitable adjustments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of compromise
Determine what the spouses agreed upon: dirhams, food, clothing, or other items. This is the first step as the ruling depends on the subject.
2
Check if the judge could have ordered that subject
If the subject is something a judge is permitted to fix as maintenance (e.g., money, food staples), the compromise is taqdir. If it is something a judge would not order (e.g., a specific garment, a slave), it is mu'awadah.
This is the core illah: the judge's authority to impose the item.
3
Determine the time of compromise relative to maintenance obligation
Was the compromise made before the maintenance became a debt (i.e., before the end of the month) or after? This affects whether the compromise is valid as taqdir or possibly invalid as debt-for-debt sale.
Maintenance becomes a debt at the end of each month.
4
Apply the illah: is maintenance a debt yet?
If not yet a debt, compromise is valid as taqdir. If debt, and the compromise is on unspecified goods, it becomes an invalid sale of debt for debt.
This is the qiyas: maintenance before due is not a debt, so taqdir is allowed.
5
State the consequence: can amount be changed?
If taqdir, either spouse may request increase or decrease based on need and capacity. If mu'awadah, the amount is binding and cannot be altered unless by mutual new agreement or special circumstances.
Exception: if the husband cannot bear the amount, the judge may reduce after inquiry.

So if this is the case in a compromise, then in the judge's fixing it is more appropriate because he has general authority. When the judge fixes for her maintenance every day, month, or year, the fixing is binding as long as she is in his marriage where no extinguisher exists and it is proportionate to their status. In Khizanat al-Muftin: If the judge wants to fix maintenance, he says 'I have fixed upon you the maintenance of your wife such and such for such and such period' or 'I have adjudged upon you maintenance for such period', it is valid and the husband is obliged, to the extent that it does not fall by the passing of time, because future maintenance becomes obligatory by the judge's decree, so that even if she remits after fixing it is valid. This is evidence for what we said that its fixing is a judicial decree and that if he fixes it and then time passes, it does not fall. It was narrated in Fath al-Qadir that she has no maintenance when the husband claims marriage and she denies it or vice versa, and he objected that this harms her, but it is an oversight because if he is denying, they negated maintenance for the period of questioning witnesses, not absolutely, with the possibility that if the judge fixes for her it is permissible. As for after the judge adjudges the marriage by evidence, there is no doubt about its obligation. It is understood from the author's conjoining of mutual consent to judicial decree that the judge's fixing is by compulsion, and we have stated that if he fixes more than his status, he may refuse the increase. Similarly, if they agree on more than the standard maintenance, as in al-Zahiriyya: If a man compromises with his wife about monthly maintenance for one hundred dirhams while the husband is in need, only the standard maintenance is binding on him; if he compromises with her for a da'iq each month, it is permissible and she may revoke if it does not suffice her. In al-Dhakhira: If a woman compromises with her husband from her maintenance for three dirhams each month, it is permissible and that is an estimation of her maintenance. The principle is that whenever a compromise between them occurs on something that a judge is permitted to fix in her maintenance, then the compromise is an estimation of maintenance and is not considered an exchange, whether this compromise occurs before the judge's fixing or mutual agreement on something or after either of them. If the compromise occurs on something that a judge is not permitted to fix on the husband in her maintenance under any circumstances, like a garment or a slave, then we consider: if the compromise was between them before the judge's decree of maintenance and before their mutual agreement on something per month, the compromise is considered an estimation; after either, it is considered an exchange. The benefit of considering it estimation is that increase and decrease are permissible; the benefit of considering it exchange is that increase or decrease are not permissible. So if he compromises with her on dirhams each month and she says 'it does not suffice me', she is increased; and if the man says 'I cannot bear it', he is not believed in that because he undertook it voluntarily, and that is evidence that he is able to fulfill what he undertook, so it is binding on him unless the judge inquires about his condition by asking people; if they inform him that he cannot bear it, he reduces it and imposes according to his capacity. If even a part of the month has not passed when he compromised with her concerning the dirhams for something, if that something is something the judge is permitted to fix, such as compromising on dirhams for three measures of specific or unspecified wheat, it is an estimation of maintenance; if it is a garment or similar, it is an exchange. This is not like debts: if a man has three dirhams against another and compromises from the dirhams for three measures of unspecified wheat, the compromise is invalid because it is an exchange since the debt was obligatory beforehand, so it would be a sale of debt for debt, which is invalid unless the wheat is delivered in the session. But here, before the month passes, maintenance does not become a debt, so it is not an exchange; rather it is an estimation of maintenance, such that if the month passes and the dirhams become a debt, then he compromises with her for unspecified wheat, it is also invalid for what we said. From this it is understood that their mutual consent and compromise on something suitable for maintenance after the judge's fixing of maintenance nullifies the judge's estimation, so that only what they mutually agree upon after the judge's fixing is binding on him. So it is deduced that if they agree that she eat with him as provisions after fixing of maintenance, or an agreement on a specific amount, it nullifies the prior estimation due to her consent, and this occurs frequently in our time. In al-Dhakhira also: If he compromises with her for a year's maintenance for a garment, it is permissible. If the garment is reclaimed (by rightful owner), then if the compromise occurred after the fixing or mutual consent, she returns to what was fixed for her or what they mutually agreed upon, because her taking the garment was a purchase and it was rescinded by the claim, so her debt returns. If it was before the fixing and mutual consent, she returns to the value of the garment. If he compromises with her for a medium slave and does not set a term or sets it, then if it was before the fixing or mutual consent it is permissible; if after either it is not permissible. The compromise of a mukataba (slave in contract of manumission) on her maintenance is permissible, like compromise on her dower, because it is her right. Similarly, a restricted slave (mahjur) if he compromises on his wife's maintenance, having married with the master's permission, and likewise the mukatab's compromise on his wife's maintenance each month is permissible a fortiori. From his statement: (And with the death of either of them the adjudged maintenance falls...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the legal effect of spousal compromise (sulh) on maintenance (nafaqah) after a judge has fixed it. The author first emphasizes that a judge's fixing of maintenance is binding and does not fall with the passage of time, even if the wife remits. He then examines the case of a compromise: if the compromise is on something the judge could have imposed (like dirhams), it is considered an estimation (taqdir) of maintenance, not an exchange (mu'awadah). This means either spouse can later request an increase or decrease based on need. If the compromise is on something the judge could not impose (like a garment), it is an exchange, binding both parties with no adjustment possible. The key distinction is whether the maintenance has become a debt at the time of compromise. Before the month ends, it is not a debt; after the month, it becomes a debt, and then a compromise on unspecified goods would be invalid as a sale of debt for debt. The passage also notes that a subsequent agreement after the judge's fixing nullifies the earlier estimation, which is a frequent occurrence in practice. Examples are given for various scenarios, including when the item is reclaimed. The author concludes with rulings on mukatabs and restricted slaves.

Key Terms
تقدير (taqdir): An estimation or fixing of maintenance by mutual agreement, where the amount is adjustable based on need.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): An exchange (like a sale) where the amount is fixed and cannot be altered thereafter.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement between spouses regarding maintenance, which may be taqdir or mu'awadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband and wife compromise on monthly maintenance of 100 dirhams. Later, the wife finds it insufficient and requests more. According to Hanafi law, can she be increased?
A) No, because the compromise is a binding exchange.
B) Yes, because the compromise is an estimation (taqdir) and can be adjusted.
C) Only if the husband agrees.
D) She can only increase after a year.

Key Takeaway
A spousal compromise on maintenance is either a flexible estimate (taqdir) or a fixed exchange (mu'awadah), determined by whether the judge could have ordered that item – a rule that balances the wife's need for adequate support with the husband's capacity.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Does Adjudged Maintenance Fall with Divorce? The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on adjudged maintenance and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether adjudged maintenance falls with divorce and apply the exception for borrowing with judge's permission.

Core Idea
Adjudged maintenance falls with death; divorce is disputed: some equate it to death, others treat it as a surviving debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced point: while it is agreed that maintenance adjudged by a court falls with the death of either spouse (since maintenance is a gift), there is a dispute within the Hanafi school about the effect of divorce. Moreover, an exception exists when the wife borrowed with the judge's permission – that debt does not fall. We will explore the reasoning and the harmonization attempted by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its rationale
Note that with the death of either spouse, adjudged maintenance falls because it is considered a gift (silah) and gifts are extinguished by death. Recognize that this rule applies whether or not the wife borrowed, unless an exception applies.
The rule is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Analyze the exception for borrowing with judge's permission
When the wife borrowed with the judge's order, that debt does not fall with death because the judge's authority is akin to the husband himself borrowing. A husband's own borrowing would survive his death.
This exception is upheld in al-Zahiriyya and al-Dhakhira.
3
Recognize the dispute regarding divorce
Some Hanafi authorities (al-Nuqaya, al-Jawhara) hold that adjudged maintenance falls with divorce just as with death, while others (al-Khaniyya, al-Zahiriyya) disagree. The author attempts to harmonize by suggesting the dispute may depend on whether a delay (tarakhi) and specific agreement were stipulated.
The text does not explicitly resolve which view is mufta bihi.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is whether a court will enforce payment of adjudged maintenance after a divorce. According to the first view, the debt falls; according to the second, it remains enforceable.
This affects the wife's right to collect unpaid maintenance.

وبموت أحدهما تسقط المقضية أي بموت أحد الزوجين تسقط النفقة المقضى بها لأن النفقة صلة والصلات تسقط بالموت كالهبة والدية والجزية وضمان العتق أطلقه فشمل ما إذا استدانت أو لا ؛ فإن كانت استدانت بغير إذن القاضي فإنها تسقط بموت أحدهما كما لو أنفقت مال نفسها، وإن كانت الاستدانة بأمر القاضي جزم في الظهيرية بعدم السقوط وصححه في الذخيرة، ونسبه إلى الكافي للحاكم الشهيد لأن للقاضي ولاية عامة بمنزلة استدانة الزوج بنفسه، ولو استدان الزوج بنفسه لا يسقط ذلك الدين بموت أحدهما كذا هذا اهـ. قيد بالموت لأن سقوط النفقة المقضى بها بالطلاق مختلف فيه فجزم في النقاية بسقوطها به كالموت مسوياً بينهما، وكذا في الجوهرة وذكر في الخانية والظهيرية : أيضاً ولا ينافي ذلك قول الذخيرة لا فرق بين أن تكون النفقة مفروضة أو لا إذ لا يلزم من عدم اشتراط فرضها من القاضي عدم اشتراط التراخي والاصطلاح على شيء معين بين الزوجين ففي اشتراط التراخي توفيق بين كلامهم أيضاً فليتأمل .
And with the death of either of them the adjudged maintenance falls, i.e., with the death of one of the spouses the maintenance that was adjudged falls, because maintenance is a gift and gifts fall with death, like gift, blood money, jizya, and guarantee of emancipation. He left it unrestricted, so it includes whether she borrowed or not. If she borrowed without the judge's permission, it falls with the death of either, just as if she spent her own money. If the borrowing was by the judge's order, it is asserted in al-Zahiriyya that it does not fall, and it was corrected in al-Dhakhira, and attributed to al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid, because the judge has general authority, like the husband borrowing himself, and if the husband himself borrowed, that debt does not fall with the death of either. So it is. He restricted it to death because the falling of adjudged maintenance by divorce is disputed. In al-Nuqaya it is asserted that it falls by divorce like death, equating the two, similarly in al-Jawhara. And it is mentioned in al-Khaniyya and al-Zahiriyya as well. And this does not contradict the statement of al-Dhakhira that there is no difference between whether the maintenance is fixed or not, for the absence of requiring its fixing by a judge does not entail the absence of requiring delay and agreement on a specific thing between the spouses. In requiring delay there is harmonization between their statements as well, so reflect.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the crucial question of whether adjudged maintenance (nafaqah muqaddah) falls with the death of either spouse or with divorce. The general rule is that maintenance is like a gift (silah), and gifts are extinguished by death, similar to hiba, diyah, jizyah, and daman al-'itq. Therefore, if the wife borrowed without the judge's permission, that debt also falls with death. However, if the wife borrowed with the judge's order, then according to the relied-upon position (as in al-Zahiriyya and al-Dhakhira), that debt does not fall with death, because the judge's order has the same effect as if the husband himself had borrowed. The husband's own borrowing would not fall with death. Regarding divorce, there is disagreement: some authorities (al-Nuqaya, al-Jawhara) hold that adjudged maintenance falls with divorce just as with death, while others (al-Khaniyya, al-Zahiriyya) do not make this equation. The author harmonizes by suggesting that perhaps the dispute concerns the requirement of delay (tarakhi) and specific agreement between spouses. The reader is urged to reflect.

Key Terms
النفقة المقضية (al-nafaqah al-muqaddah): Maintenance that has been adjudicated and fixed by a judge.
صلة (silah): A gift or grant that is not a reciprocal exchange; maintenance is considered silah.
استدانة (istidanah): Borrowing; in context, the wife borrowing to support herself with or without judge's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in al-Zahiriyya and al-Dhakhira, what happens when a wife borrows with the judge's permission and then the husband dies?
A) The borrowed amount falls with the husband's death like regular maintenance.
B) The borrowed amount does not fall because it is treated as the husband's own debt.
C) Half of the borrowed amount falls.
D) The borrowed amount is deducted from the wife's inheritance.

Key Takeaway
Adjudged maintenance falls with death, but divorce is disputed; a debt arising from borrowing with judge's permission survives death or divorce.

Effect of Death on Decreed Maintenance
Explains the ruling on death of a spouse and the exception for borrowing with judge's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on decreed maintenance upon the death of a spouse, explain the exception for borrowing with the judge's permission, and apply the qiyas of silah.

Core Idea
Decreed maintenance drops with death because it is a silah, but a judge's order to borrow transforms it into a debt that survives.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi family law: the fate of decreed maintenance (nafaqah muqaddiyah) when a spouse dies. While the default ruling is that it drops, the text examines an exception when the wife borrowed with the judge's permission, using analogical reasoning (qiyas) to liken such borrowing to the husband's own debt. Understanding this nuance helps prevent confusion between debts that survive death and obligations that are contingent on the marital relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on decreed maintenance (nafaqah muqaddiyah) and states that it drops upon the death of either spouse.
The ruling is absolute: includes both husband's and wife's death.
2
Locate the dalil
The author classifies maintenance as a 'silah' (ongoing support) and argues by analogy that silah drops with death, like gifts (hibah), blood-money (diyah), jizyah, and guarantee of emancipation (daman al-itiq).
This is a qiyas based on the common property of being a silah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal reason (illah) is that silah is not a fixed debt until received; it is contingent on an ongoing relationship. Death severs that relationship, so the obligation ceases.
Hence, the maintenance obligation is extinguished even if it was decreed.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether borrowing with the judge's permission prevents dropping. Al-Zahiriya and al-Dhakhira hold that it does not drop, while other views may differ.
The ikhtilaf is only on the exception, not the default ruling.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that borrowing with judge's permission transforms the maintenance into a debt analogous to the husband's own borrowing, hence it survives death.
This is preferred to prevent hardship for the wife who incurred debt on the court's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبموت أحدهما تسقط المقضية ) أي بموت أحد الزوجين تسقط النفقة المقضى بها لأن النفقة صلة والصلات تسقط بالموت كالهبة والدية والجزية وضمان العتق أطلقه فشمل ما إذا استدانت أو لا ؛ فإن كانت استدانت بغير إذن القاضي فإنها تسقط بموت أحدهما كما لو أنفقت مال نفسها، وإن كانت الاستدانة بأمر القاضي جزم في الظهيرية بعدم السقوط وصححه في الذخيرة، ونسبه إلى الكافي للحاكم الشهيد لأن للقاضي ولاية عامة بمنزلة استدانة الزوج بنفسه، ولو استدان الزوج بنفسه لا يسقط ذلك الدين بموت أحدهما كذا هذا اهـ. قيد بالموت لأن سقوط النفقة المقضى بها بالطلاق مختلف فيه فجزم في النقاية بسقوطها به كالموت مسوياً بينهما، وكذا في الجوهرة وذكر في الخانية والظهيرية : أيضاً ولا ينافي ذلك قول الذخيرة لا فرق بين أن تكون النفقة مفروضة أو لا إذ لا يلزم من عدم اشتراط فرضها من القاضي عدم اشتراط التراخي والاصطلاح على شيء معين بين الزوجين ففي اشتراط التراخي توفيق بين كلامهم أيضاً فليتأمل.
His saying: (And by the death of one of them, the decreed maintenance drops) meaning by the death of one of the two spouses, the maintenance that has been decreed drops because maintenance is a connection (silah) and connections drop with death, like gift, blood-money, jizyah, and guarantee of emancipation. He gave it absolutely so it includes whether she borrowed or not. If she borrowed without the permission of the judge, then it drops by the death of one of them just as if she spent her own wealth. But if the borrowing was by the order of the judge, it is firmly stated in al-Zahiriya that it does not drop, and it was corrected in al-Dhakhira, and attributed to al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid, because the judge has general authority, like the husband borrowing himself. And if the husband borrowed himself, that debt does not drop by the death of one of them, likewise this. End. He restricted it to death because the dropping of decreed maintenance by divorce is disputed. In al-Niqaya it is firmly stated that it drops by it (divorce) like death, making no distinction between them, and likewise in al-Jawhara. And it is mentioned in al-Khaniya and al-Zahiriya also. And that does not contradict the statement of al-Dhakhira that there is no difference between whether maintenance is decreed or not, because lack of condition of its being decreed by the judge does not necessitate lack of condition of deferment and agreement on a specific thing between the spouses. So regarding the condition of deferment, there is harmonization between their statements. So reflect.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof, explaining the effect of death on decreed maintenance (nafaqah muqaddiyah). It establishes that such maintenance drops upon the death of either spouse because it is a 'silah' — a form of ongoing support that, like gifts (hibah) and jizyah, ceases with death before actual receipt. However, an exception is highlighted: if the wife borrowed money with the judge's permission to cover maintenance, that sum does not drop because the judge's order analogizes it to the husband's own borrowing, which remains a debt. The author then notes that the restriction to death in the base text implies that divorce does not necessarily cause dropping, and there is disagreement on that point. Some Hanafi sources (al-Niqaya, al-Jawhara) equate divorce with death in dropping maintenance, while others do not. The author leaves the matter for reflection (ta'ammul).

Key Terms
النفقة المقضى بها (al-nafaqah al-muqaddā bihā): Maintenance that has been decreed by a judge for the wife, as opposed to maintenance that has not yet been decreed.
صلة (ṣilah): A form of ongoing support or connection that is not a debt until fixed; it drops with death before receipt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It remains as a debt and does not drop.
D) It becomes a gift and is forgiven.

Key Takeaway
Decreed maintenance drops upon death because it is a silah, but if the wife borrowed with the judge's permission, it becomes a debt that survives death.

Does Divorce Drop Decreed Maintenance? Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether divorce drops decreed maintenance, followed by a critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two contrasting Hanafi positions on whether decreed maintenance drops with divorce, explain the key analogy and the three objections, and apply the preponderant view in a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Decreed maintenance drops with divorce according to later Hanafis, but a strong critique says it does not.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: does the accumulated maintenance owed by a husband drop when he divorces his wife? The later preponderant view, supported by an analogy with the jizyah of a dhimmi who converts, holds that it does. However, a later scholar, al-Abd al-Da'if, raises powerful objections that question the soundness of this ruling. Understanding both sides deepens your appreciation of Hanafi legal reasoning and the process of tarjih (weighting of evidence).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Some Hanafis (e.g., al-Nasafi, the two shaykhs) hold that decreed maintenance drops with divorce, just as it drops with death. Others (some earlier scholars and al-Abd al-Da'if) argue it does not drop.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; no other schools are referenced.
2
Examine the evidence for dropping
The main evidence is an analogy (qiyas) with the jizyah of a dhimmi: when the dhimmi converts to Islam, the accumulated jizyah drops because the basis for it (persistence in false religion) ceases. Similarly, marriage maintenance is based on the marital tie (waslah), which is severed by divorce.
This analogy is used by the proponents of dropping.
3
Analyze the three objections of al-Abd al-Da'if
He argues: (1) If maintenance dropped, a husband could divorce to avoid payment then take his wife back; (2) Surety for decreed maintenance is valid, proving it is a regular debt that only drops by payment or discharge; (3) In khul', divorce for compensation does not drop marriage rights, implying that ordinary divorce should not drop maintenance either.
The third objection is considered the strongest.
4
Assess the qualification of al-Ramli
Al-Ramli restricts the dropping to cases where at least a month has passed since the decree, implying that if the divorce occurs soon after, the maintenance might not drop.
This qualification indicates the issue is not fully settled.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
Despite the strong critique, the later authorities (al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Marghinani) gave fatwa that it drops. Thus, the mufta bihi in the school is that decreed maintenance drops with divorce, but the ikhtilaf remains legitimate.
The student should recognize the dispute and the reasoning on both sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يوسف. وذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني زاد الخصاف لسقوط النفقة المفروضة سبباً آخر فقال : تسقط بموته وموتها وتسقط إذا طلقها أو أبانها اهـ. هذه عبارتهما باللفظ وفي الخلاصة والبزازية : وهل تسقط النفقة المفروضة الطلاق الرجعي والبائن لأنه في عبارة الخانية والظهيرية قد عطف البائن على الطلاق فعلم أن الطلاق قال العبد الضعيف : ينبغي ضعف القول بسقوطها بالطلاق ولو بائناً لأمور : الأول أنهم اتفقوا على أنه يحبس في And just as the decreed maintenance drops with the death of one of the spouses, does it drop with divorce? They differed regarding it. Some said it does not drop. And Qadi Imam Abu Ali al-Nasafi said: I found a riwaya (transmission) of dropping. And al-Baqqali mentioned that according to Muhammad it drops, and there is no riwaya from Abu Yusuf. And Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, adding al-Khassaf, mentioned another cause for dropping of decreed maintenance: he said it drops with his death and her death, and it drops if he divorces her or irrevocably separates her. End. These are their statements verbatim. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziya: And does the decreed maintenance drop with divorce? It is narrated from Qadi Imam Abu Ali al-Nasafi that it drops, and in the Fatawa of al-Baqqali the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is mentioned. End. And in al-Dhakhira: If the husband divorces her in this situation, what has accumulated of maintenance after the judge's decree drops; thus it is narrated from Qadi Imam Abu Ali al-Nasafi. And he used to say: We found a riwaya of this issue in the book of the judge, and by it the Sadr al-Shahid and Shaykh Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani gave fatwa. And he analogized it to the dhimmi if the head-tax (kharaj al-ra's) has accumulated on him, then he becomes Muslim, it drops from him what had accumulated. And the aspect of the analogy is that the dhimmi only took the jizyah for his persistence on the false religion, and that meaning ceased with Islam, so the jizyah drops. Similarly here, the woman is only entitled to maintenance due to the connection (waslah) that existed between them, and that connection has been severed by divorce. But if the maintenance was borrowed by the judge's order, it does not drop by divorce, and that is the correct view because it is like the husband borrowing himself. End of what is in al-Dhakhira. And in al-Mujtaba: And if the husband divorces her in these ways, then what has accumulated upon him after the judge's decree drops. End. So it has become clear that the preponderant (rajih) view among them is that it drops with divorce like death, especially since the two shaykhs gave fatwa with it as in al-Dhakhira, and the apparent meaning of their statements is that there is no difference between revocable (raj'i) and irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, because in the expression of al-Khaniya and al-Zahiriya, the ba'in is conjoined to divorce, so it is known that divorce... Al-Abd al-Da'if said: It should be considered weak (da'if) the opinion of dropping by divorce, even if ba'in, for several reasons: First, they agreed that he is imprisoned for decreed maintenance if he refrains from paying it, but if it dropped by divorce, he could divorce her to drop it, then take her back. Second, they explicitly allowed taking a surety (kafil) for decreed maintenance for the period the judge decreed, even though surety is only valid for a valid debt. They said: It is something that only drops by payment or discharge. So if the debt of maintenance dropped by divorce, it would not be valid, so surety for it would not be valid. And it does not harm us that it drops by death of one of them, because that is due to an incidental cause, since its origin is silah, and silah drops by death before receipt. Third, and it is the strongest: what they mentioned in the chapter of khul': all of them mentioned that divorce for compensation does not drop any of the rights of marriage, contrary to khul' for compensation. And there is no harm in mentioning his saying: (He restricted it to death...) said al-Ramli: My shaykh, Shaykh Muhammad ibn Siraj al-Hanuti, restricted the dropping by divorce to when a month has passed, meaning or more, and it is a necessary restriction. Reflect.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a wife's decreed maintenance is dropped by divorce (talaq). It begins by noting the disagreement: some say it does not drop; Abu Ali al-Nasafi found a riwaya supporting dropping; al-Baqqali reports that Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds it drops, with no riwaya from Abu Yusuf. Al-Halwani includes irrevocable separation (ibana) as causing dropping. The Dhakhira similarly quotes al-Nasafi and the fatwa of al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Marghinani, drawing an analogy with jizyah dropping upon a dhimmi's conversion. The preponderant view (rajih) among later scholars is that it drops, and they make no distinction between revocable and irrevocable divorce. However, a later commentator (al-Abd al-Da'if) argues forcefully that this view is weak. He gives three reasons: (1) If it dropped, a husband could simply divorce to evade payment and then take her back; (2) the validity of surety (kafalah) for decreed maintenance shows it is a debt that does not drop without payment or discharge—death drops it only because of its nature as silah; (3) in khul', divorce for compensation does not extinguish marital rights, indicating that ordinary divorce should not extinguish the obligation of maintenance. The commentator also mentions al-Ramli's qualification that dropping applies only after a month has passed. This lesson thus presents both sides of the dispute and the reasoning behind each.

Key Terms
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-rajʿī): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back the wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.
الطلاق البائن (al-ṭalāq al-bāʾin): Irrevocable divorce; the wife is completely separated and cannot be taken back except with a new marriage contract.
صلة (ṣilah): A form of continuous support that is not a fixed debt; it drops with death or cessation of the relationship.
الفرض (al-farḍ): Decree or judicial determination; here, the judge's order of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Abd al-Da'if's critique, which of the following is a reason to reject the view that decreed maintenance drops with divorce?
A) The husband would have no incentive to pay if he could divorce and then remarry.
B) It is impossible to appoint a surety for such maintenance.
C) In the chapter of khul', divorce for compensation does not drop rights of marriage.
D) The Quran prohibits dropping any rights upon divorce.

Key Takeaway
Whether decreed maintenance drops with divorce is a contested issue in the Hanafi school; the later preponderant view holds it drops, but strong objections question that ruling, and al-Ramli adds a temporal qualification.

Sale of a Slave for Nafaqah: Conditions and Qiyas
Expands the ruling with conditions and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a fully owned slave can be sold for his wife's maintenance and apply the Hanafi qiyas from the trading slave case.

Core Idea
A slave can be sold for his wife's nafaqah only if married with permission; it is analogized to a trade debt.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi ruling that a male slave (qinn) may be sold to satisfy his wife's nafaqah. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the key conditions—especially the master's permission for marriage—and the legal analogy (qiyas) with a trading slave's debt. We will explore the master's option to ransom the slave and the wife's alternative to demand istis'a' (working to pay).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states: 'The male slave is sold for the nafaqah of his wife.' The default ruling is that such a sale is valid (sahih). Determine the conditions that must be met.
The ruling is not unconditional; it depends on several factors.
2
Locate the primary condition: master's permission
The commentary specifies that the marriage must have been contracted with the master's permission. Without it, the marriage is not effective (nufadh), so no nafaqah is obligatory.
'Permission' here means authorization to marry; without it, the marriage is suspended.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) with a trading slave
The passage likens the nafaqah debt to a trade debt incurred by a ma'dhun (authorized trading slave). In both cases, the debt becomes apparent in the master's right and attaches to the slave's person (raqabah).
The 'illa (effective cause) is that both debts are financial obligations that arise legitimately and affect the master's property.
4
Note the master's right to ransom
The master may ransom the slave by paying the wife's nafaqah instead of letting him be sold. This is because her right is in the nafaqah, not in the specific person.
If the slave dies or is killed, the debt drops; it is like a gift (silah).
5
Consider the wife's option of istis'a'
The wife may choose to have the slave work to pay the nafaqah instead of demanding his sale. This is derived by analogy from the similar option given to creditors of a trading slave.
The commentary states it should be the same here, even for daily nafaqah.

Source Text
