When Must a Husband Provide a Female Companion for His Wife?
Conditional obligation of mu'nisah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that make providing a mu'nisah wajib for the husband and apply the ruling to different dwelling scenarios.

Core Idea
A wife's companion is not always required—it depends on the dwelling, neighbors, and her fear of harm.

The Hanafi school discusses a unique obligation: the husband may need to provide a female companion (mu'nisah) for his wife to prevent loneliness, especially when he is away. This ruling is not absolute; it hinges on the wife's dwelling, the availability of neighbors, and her mental well-being. The passage reconciles differing views within the school, giving us a nuanced conditional rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default position
The default ruling, stated in Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah, is that a mu'nisah is not obligatory if the husband houses his wife among righteous people so she does not feel lonely. This implies the companion is needed when she would indeed feel lonely.
The phrase 'so she does not feel lonely' is key.
2
Identify the trigger for obligation
The companion becomes wajib when the wife would suffer loneliness (istihash) due to a large, isolated dwelling or distant neighbors. The commentators emphasize that even with neighbors, if the house is spacious or she fears for her sanity, providing a companion is wajib.
The principle of preventing harm (ḍirār) underlies this obligation.
3
Reconcile the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Qari' al-Hidayah's non-obligation is reconciled: it applies only to small dwellings where neighbors can quickly respond. For larger homes or when the wife feels lonely despite neighbors, the companion is wajib.
This reconciliation is from al-Shurnublali and al-Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud.
4
Apply the refined rule to personal factors
The ruling also depends on the wife's personal situation: a young wife with co-wives may need a companion even in a small house with neighbors, because co-wife jealousy can cause mental harm.
The maxim 'no harm in Islam' (la ḍirār wa lā ḍirār) is the basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر المؤنسة إلا أنه في فتاوى قارئ الهداية قال : إنها لا تجب ويسكنها بين قوم صالحين بحيث لا تستوحش وهو ظاهر في وجوبها فيما إذا كان البيت خالياً من الجيران ولا سيما إذا كانت تخشى على عقلها من سعته اهـ. ونظر فيه في الشرنبلالية بما ذكره المؤلف من قوله قد علم من أن البيت الذي ليس له جيران غير مسكن شرعي. وقال السيد أبو السعود أقول: ما ذكره قارئ الهداية من عدم اللزوم يحمل على ما إذا كان المسكن صغيراً كالمساكن التي في الربوع والحيشان يشير إلى ذلك قوله : بحيث لا تستوحش إذ لا يلزم من كون المسكن بين جيران عدم لزوم الإتيان بالمؤنسة إذا استوحشت بأن كان المسكن متسعاً كالدار، وإن كان لها جيران فعدم الإتيان بالمؤنسة في هذه الحالة لا شك أنه من المضارة لا سيما إذا خشيت على عقلها وما في النهر من قوله «وهو ظاهر في وجوبها إذا كان المسكن خالياً عن الجيران يحمل على ما إذا رضيت بإسكانها فيه ولم تطالبه بالمسكن الشرعي وهو ما له جيران وحينئذ فلا يستقيم الرد عليه بما في البحر فتحصل أن الإتيان بالمؤنسة وعدمه يختلف باختلاف المساكن ولو مع وجود الجيران، فإن كان المسكن بحال لو استغاثت بجيرانها أغاثوها سريعاً لما بينهم من القرب لا تلزمه المؤنسة وإلا لزمته .اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون أيضاً مختلفاً باختلاف الناس فإن بعض النساء تستوحش في البيتوتة في البيت ولو صغيراً بين جيران إذا كان زوجها له زوجة أخرى أو أكثر فإذا كان يخشى على عقلها إذا كانت ليلة ضرتها ينبغي أن يؤمر بالمؤنسة ولا سيما إذا كانت صغيرة فإن كثيراً من الرجال لا يمكنه أن يبيت وحده فكيف النساء ولا ضرار في الشرع
He mentioned the companion, except that in Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah he said: It is not obligatory, and he houses her among righteous people such that she does not feel lonely. And this implies its obligation when the house is empty of neighbors, especially if she fears for her sanity due to its spaciousness. End. And al-Shurnublaliyah considered this with what the author mentioned from his statement: it has been learned that a house without neighbors is not a lawful residence. Al-Sayyid Abū al-Su‘ūd said: I say: What Qari' al-Hidayah mentioned of non-obligation is carried upon when the dwelling is small, like dwellings in quarters and courtyards. His statement 'such that she does not feel lonely' indicates that, for the presence of the dwelling among neighbors does not negate the necessity of providing a companion if she feels lonely because the dwelling is spacious like a house. And if she has neighbors, not providing a companion in this case is undoubtedly harmful, especially if she fears for her sanity. What is in al-Nahr from his statement 'it is apparent in its obligation when the dwelling is empty of neighbors' is carried upon when she agrees to be housed in it and does not demand the lawful residence which has neighbors. Then the objection against what is in al-Bahr is not valid. Thus, providing a companion or not varies with different dwellings, even with the presence of neighbors. If the dwelling is such that if she calls for help her neighbors would respond quickly due to proximity, the companion is not obligatory; otherwise, it is. End. It should also vary with different people, for some women feel lonely staying in a house even if it is small and among neighbors, if her husband has another wife or more. So if one fears for her sanity on the night of her co-wife, he should be ordered to provide a companion, especially if she is young. Indeed, many men cannot sleep alone, so how about women? And there is no harming in Islamic law.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that refines the obligation of providing a female companion (mu'nisah) for the wife. The initial ruling from Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah is that a companion is not obligatory if the husband houses her among righteous neighbors so she does not feel lonely. However, the commentator shows that this implies obligation when the house is isolated, especially if spacious. Al-Shurnublaliyah reconciles the statements: the non-obligation applies to small dwellings like rooms in a shared complex, where neighbors are close. But if the dwelling is large, even with neighbors, loneliness may require a companion. The decisive view from al-Nahr and al-Bahr is that the ruling depends on the specific dwelling: if neighbors are far and cannot respond quickly, the companion becomes wajib. Additionally, the ruling varies with the woman's circumstances; a young wife with co-wives may need a companion to prevent harm to her mental health. The principle is that causing harm (ḍirār) is prohibited in Islam.

Key Terms
المؤنسة (al-mu’nisah): A female companion provided by the husband to keep the wife company and prevent loneliness when he is away.
الاستيحاش (al-istīḥāsh): Feeling lonely or fearful due to being alone, especially in a large house.
المضارة (al-muḍārrah): Causing harm to the wife, which is prohibited in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only when the house has no neighbors.
B) When the wife would suffer loneliness or harm to her sanity, even if neighbors are present.
C) Whenever the husband travels, regardless of dwelling.
D) Only if the wife is young and has co-wives.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of a mu'nisah hinges on preventing harm: it is wajib when the wife would suffer loneliness, especially in large isolated dwellings or when her mental health is at risk.

Husband's Right to Prevent Wife's Relatives from Entering His House
Discussing different opinions on whether the husband can forbid the wife's relatives from visiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the operative Hanafi ruling on the husband's right to prevent his wife's parents and mahrams from visiting, and identify which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Core Idea
The husband may not bar parents from visiting weekly nor other mahrams yearly; only prolonged stays can be prevented.

Islamic law balances the husband's authority over his home with the prohibition of severing kinship ties. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the husband can prevent his wife's relatives from entering his house. The text weighs multiple opinions and settles on a nuanced position that preserves family bonds while preventing fitna.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the central question? The text asks whether the husband can prevent the wife's parents and mahrams from entering his house or the wife from visiting them.
2
Recognize the weaker views
Two earlier positions are mentioned: (1) he can prevent entry entirely because the house is his property; (2) he can only prevent them from staying (al-qarār) due to fitna.
These views are not relied upon.
3
State the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ)
The relied-upon view is that he cannot prevent parents from visiting every Friday, nor other mahrams once a year. He can only prevent them from staying (al-kinūna). The fatwa is on this view.
From al-Khaniyya.
4
Consider Abu Yusuf's qualification
In al-Nawadir, Abu Yusuf adds that the wife should not go out to her parents if they are able to come to her. This is considered good (ḥasan).
Some mashayikh adopted this as the preferred view.
5
Determine the operative fatwa
The text concludes that the truth is to adopt Abu Yusuf's view when parents can visit; otherwise, she may visit occasionally. The general fatwa remains the ṣaḥīḥ view allowing weekly parental entry.
Frequency adjusted for fitna concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولهم النظر والكلام معها) يعني في أي وقت اختار أهلها ذلك فلهم ذلك لما عدمه من قطيعة الرحم وليس له في ذلك ضرر، وقد أفاد كلامه أن له أن يمنع أهلها من الدخول في بيته ولو والدة أو ولداً لأن المنزل ملكه وله حق المنع من الدخول في ملكه . وأما القيام على باب الدار فليس له منعهم منه كالكلام كما في الخانية واختاره القدوري، وقيل لا يمنعهم من الدخول وإنما يمنعهم من القرار لأن الفتنة في المكث وطول الكلام. والصحيح خلاف كل من القولين قالوا الصحيح أنه لا يمنعها من الخروج إلى الوالدين ولا يمنعهما من الدخول عليها في كل جمعة وفي غيرهما من المحارم في كل سنة، وإنما يمنعهم من الكينونة عندها وعليه الفتوى كما في الخانية. وعن أبي يوسف في النوادر تقييد خروجها بأن لا يقدرا على اتيانها، فإن كانا يقدران على اتيانها لا تذهب وهو حسن فإن بعض النساء لا يشق عليها مع الأب الخروج وقد يشق ذلك على الزوج فتمتنع وقد اختار بعض المشايخ منعها من الخروج إليهما وقد أشار إلى نقله في شرح المختار والحق الأخذ بقول أبي يوسف إذا كان الأبوان بالصفة التي ذكرت وإن لم يكونا كذلك ينبغي أن يؤذن لها في زيارتهما الحين بعد الحين على قدر متعارف أما في كل جمعة فبعيد فإن في كثرة الخروج فتح باب الفتنة خصوصاً إذا كانت شابة والزوج من ذوي الهيآت بخلاف خروج الأبوين فإنه أيسر
His statement: 'And they have the right to see and speak with her' means that whenever her family choose that, they have it, due to the absence of severing of kinship, and he has no harm in that. His statement has indicated that he has the right to prevent her family from entering his house, even a mother or a child, because the house is his property and he has the right to prevent entry into his property. As for standing at the door of the house, he cannot prevent them from that, nor from speaking, as in al-Khaniyya, and al-Quduri chose it. And it is said he does not prevent them from entering, but rather prevents them from staying (al-qarār) because the fitna is in remaining and lengthy speech. The correct view is contrary to both of these sayings. They said: The correct view is that he does not prevent her from going out to her parents, nor does he prevent them from entering upon her every Friday, and for other mahrams once every year. Rather, he prevents them from staying (al-kinūna) with her, and upon that is the fatwa as in al-Khaniyya. And from Abu Yusuf in al-Nawadir: her going out is restricted to when they (parents) are unable to come to her. If they are able to come, she does not go. This is good, for some women do not find it difficult to go out with the father, but that may be difficult for the husband, so she refrains. Some mashayikh chose to prevent her from going out to them, as indicated in Sharh al-Mukhtar. The truth is to adopt the view of Abu Yusuf when the parents are as described. If they are not so, then permission should be given for her to visit them from time to time according to custom. As for every Friday, that is unlikely, for frequent going out opens the door to fitna, especially if she is young and the husband is a man of dignity. Unlike the going out of the parents, which is easier.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the husband's right to prevent the wife's relatives from entering his house. The text first presents a statement from the matn that the wife's family has the right to see and speak with her. The commentator then clarifies that the husband can prevent entry, but not standing at the door or talking. Two weaker views are mentioned: one allows prevention of entry, another allows prevention of staying only. The correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ) is that the husband cannot prevent parents from visiting every Friday, nor prevent the wife from going out to them; he can only prevent them from staying too long. This is the fatwa. Then Abu Yusuf's view is cited from al-Nawadir: if parents can come, the wife should not go out. Some mashayikh adopted preventing her going out. The preferred view is to follow Abu Yusuf when parents are able to visit. Otherwise, she may visit occasionally according to custom, but weekly visits are not required due to fitna risks.

Key Terms
قطيعة الرحم (qaṭī‘at al-raḥim): Severance of kinship ties, which is prohibited in Islam and thus influences visitation rights.
الكينونة (al-kinūna): Staying or residing with the wife; the husband can prevent this but not brief visits.
الفتنة (al-fitna): Temptation or social discord, a reason to limit frequency of visits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa, how often may the wife's parents visit her?
A) Whenever they wish, without restriction.
B) Every Friday (once a week).
C) Only once a year.
D) Only if they are not able to come to her, then once a month.

Key Takeaway
The husband cannot prevent the wife's parents from visiting weekly, nor other mahrams yearly; he can only prevent them from staying too long.

intermediate
7 min
The Obligation of Providing a Companion (Mu'nisah) for the Wife
Intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on providing a mu'nisah to different dwelling and personal circumstances, and explain the reasoning behind the obligation.

Core Idea
A mu'nisah is required only when the wife is at risk of harm from loneliness, considering dwelling size and neighbor responsiveness.

The marital home is not just a physical shelter; it must also provide emotional security. This lesson examines when a husband must provide a female companion (mu'nisah) for his wife to prevent loneliness and harm. You will follow the reasoning of Hanafi jurists as they reconcile differing views based on the specific conditions of the dwelling and the wife's personal situation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage asks: Is a husband required to provide a mu'nisah for his wife? Note that this is a matter of nafaqah (maintenance) and prevention of harm (darar).
2
Examine the disagreement
Qari al-Hidayah said mu'nisah is not obligatory unless the house lacks neighbors. The author's commentary initially appears to critique this, saying a dwelling without neighbors is not a proper shar'i home.
Pay attention to how different conditions are weighed.
3
Apply Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud's reconciliation
He explains that Qari al-Hidayah's 'non-obligation' applies only to small dwellings where the wife does not feel lonely. In large houses (dar), even with neighbors, loneliness may still occur, making the mu'nisah obligatory.
'Large' here refers to spaciousness, not just physical size.
4
Determine the final rule
The obligation depends on whether neighbors can quickly come to her aid. If yes, no mu'nisah; if no, obligatory. Also, the wife's personal temperament (e.g., jealousy from co-wives) influences the ruling.
The principle of la darar (no harm) is the ultimate guide.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a wife living in an apartment with responsive neighbors: no mu'nisah needed. But if the apartment is isolated or the wife is prone to anxiety, it becomes wajib.
The ruling is flexible based on 'urf (custom) and individual need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامهم ذكر المؤنسة إلا أنه في فتاوى قارئ الهداية قال : إنها لا تجب ويسكنها بين قوم صالحين بحيث لا تستوحش وهو ظاهر في وجوبها فيما إذا كان البيت خالياً من الجيران ولا سيما إذا كانت تخشى على عقلها من سعته اهـ. ونظر فيه في الشرنبلالية بما ذكره المؤلف من قوله قد علم من أن البيت الذي ليس له جيران غير مسكن شرعي. وقال السيد أبو السعود أقول: ما ذكره قارئ الهداية من عدم اللزوم يحمل على ما إذا كان المسكن صغيراً كالمساكن التي في الربوع والحيشان يشير إلى ذلك قوله : بحيث لا تستوحش إذ لا يلزم من كون المسكن بين جيران عدم لزوم الإتيان بالمؤنسة إذا استوحشت بأن كان المسكن متسعاً كالدار، وإن كان لها جيران فعدم الإتيان بالمؤنسة في هذه الحالة لا شك أنه من المضارة لا سيما إذا خشيت على عقلها وما في النهر من قوله «وهو ظاهر في وجوبها إذا كان المسكن خالياً عن الجيران يحمل على ما إذا رضيت بإسكانها فيه ولم تطالبه بالمسكن الشرعي وهو ما له جيران وحينئذ فلا يستقيم الرد عليه بما في البحر فتحصل أن الإتيان بالمؤنسة وعدمه يختلف باختلاف المساكن ولو مع وجود الجيران، فإن كان المسكن بحال لو استغاثت بجيرانها أغاثوها سريعاً لما بينهم من القرب لا تلزمه المؤنسة وإلا لزمته .اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون أيضاً مختلفاً باختلاف الناس فإن بعض النساء تستوحش في البيتوتة في البيت ولو صغيراً بين جيران إذا كان زوجها له زوجة أخرى أو أكثر فإذا كان يخشى على عقلها إذا كانت ليلة ضرتها ينبغي أن يؤمر بالمؤنسة ولا سيما إذا كانت صغيرة فإن كثيراً من الرجال لا يمكنه أن يبيت وحده فكيف النساء ولا ضرار في الشرع
Their speech mentioning the mu'nisah except that in Fatawa Qari al-Hidayah, he said: It is not obligatory, and he houses her among righteous people such that she does not feel lonely. And it is apparent that it is obligatory when the house is devoid of neighbors, especially if it is feared for her mind due to its spaciousness. End quote. And al-Shurnbulaliyah commented on it by what the author mentioned from his statement: It is known that a house which has no neighbors is not a shar'i dwelling. And Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud said: I say: What Qari al-Hidayah mentioned about non-obligation is carried on the case when the dwelling is small, like dwellings in quarters and courtyards, indicated by his statement: such that she does not feel lonely. Because it is not necessary that the dwelling being among neighbors negates the obligation of bringing a companion if she feels lonely, as when the dwelling is spacious like a house. Even if she has neighbors, not bringing a companion in this situation is undoubtedly harmful, especially if it is feared for her mind. And what is in al-Nahr from his statement: 'It is apparent that it is obligatory when the dwelling is devoid of neighbors' is carried on when she is satisfied with being housed there and does not demand the shar'i dwelling (which is one with neighbors). Then the refutation in al-Bahr is not valid. So the conclusion is: bringing a mu'nisah or not varies according to dwellings, even with the presence of neighbors. If the dwelling is such that if she calls for help, her neighbors would quickly come to her aid due to proximity, then the mu'nisah is not obligatory; otherwise it is obligatory. End quote. And it should also vary according to individuals, for some women feel lonely staying in a house, even small, among neighbors if her husband has another wife or more. So if it is feared for her mind when it is the night of her co-wife, he should be ordered to bring a mu'nisah, especially if she is young. Indeed many men cannot spend the night alone, so how about women? And there is no harm in the Shari'ah.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of the husband providing a female companion (mu'nisah) for his wife to prevent loneliness and harm. The discussion arises from the base text presumably. The commentator presents the view of Qari al-Hidayah that a mu'nisah is not obligatory unless the house has no neighbors and the wife fears for her mind due to spaciousness. Then al-Shurnbulali critiqued this based on the principle that a dwelling without neighbors is not a proper shar'i dwelling. Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud reconciles by saying that Qari al-Hidayah's non-obligation applies to small dwellings where the wife does not feel lonely. If the dwelling is spacious (like a dar), even with neighbors, she may feel lonely, so the husband must provide a companion. The final ruling is that the obligation depends on the circumstances: if neighbors can quickly respond, no mu'nisah; if not, then it is obligatory. Furthermore, the individual condition of the wife matters: some women are more prone to loneliness, especially if the husband has multiple wives. The guiding principle is preventing harm (la dirara).

Key Terms
المؤنسة (al-mu'nisah): A female companion provided to keep the wife company and prevent loneliness.
المسكن الشرعي (al-maskan al-shar'i): A legally sufficient dwelling that is not isolated; typically one with neighbors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation is the husband NOT required to provide a mu'nisah (companion) for his wife?
A) The house is spacious and neighbors are far
B) The wife feels lonely due to the husband having another wife
C) The house is small and neighbors can quickly come to her aid
D) The wife is young and fears for her mind

Key Takeaway
The obligation of providing a mu'nisah is contingent on the risk of harm from loneliness, assessed by dwelling conditions and the wife's personal situation, in line with the Shari'ah's prohibition of harm.

Wife's Right to Leave the Home: A Comprehensive Hanafi Ruling
Compiles rulings on wife's outings from multiple Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a wife's outings into permitted (without permission), permitted (with permission), and prohibited, and apply the exceptions from the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A wife can visit parents weekly and maharim yearly without permission; visiting strangers is prohibited even with permission.

The Hanafi fiqh provides a nuanced set of rules governing a married woman's mobility. These rules balance the husband's rights with the wife's religious and familial obligations. In this lesson, we examine the detailed rulings from classical Hanafi sources, covering when a wife may leave the home without her husband's permission, when she needs it, and when she must obey even if she wants to go out.

Guided Path
1
Classify the outing by category
Determine whether the outing is to a family member (parent, mahram), a stranger, or for a specific purpose (midwife, knowledge, Hajj). Each category has different rules.
Family includes parents, grandparents, children, siblings, in-laws (maharim). Strangers are non-mahram.
2
Apply the frequency rule
For family: parents may be visited every Friday without permission; maharim once a year without permission. Additional visits require permission.
These frequencies are from al-Zahiriyyah and al-Khulasah.
3
Identify prohibited outings
Visiting strangers, attending weddings, going to the bathhouse, and general knowledge gatherings (unless the husband cannot answer) are prohibited even if the husband permits.
If the husband permits a prohibited outing, both sin.
4
Evaluate special cases
Consider breastfeeding a previous child (husband may prevent), caring for disabled father (wife must disobey), midwife/corpse-washer (husband may prevent), knowledge (limited), and obligatory Hajj (wife may go without permission).
The husband's right over beauty and service yields to fard 'ayn.
5
Consider the effect of mahr and seclusion
Before receiving her mahr, the wife may go out for needs and relatives without permission. A secluded woman (mukhaddarah) cannot go to court herself; she must send a representative.
These rulings are from al-Fatawa and al-Khaniyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو والدة أو ولدا قال الرملي أقول : لو كان لها ولد من غيره وأرادت أن ترضعه وتربيه هل له منعها؟ والذي يجب أن يقال إن له منعها يدل عليه ما في التتارخانية عن الكافي في إجارة الظئر : وللزوج أن يمنع امرأته عما يوجب خللاً في حقه . وما فيها أيضاً نقلاً. السغناقي: ولأنها في الإرضاع والسهر تتعب وذلك ينقص جمالها وجمالها حق الزوج فكان له أن يمنعها تأمل اه ولو كان أبوها زمناً مثلاً وهو يحتاج إلى خدمتها والزوج يمنعها من تعاهده فعليها أن تعصيه مسلماً كان الأب أو كافراً؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد استفيد مما ذكرناه أن لها الخروج إلى زيارة الأبوين والمحارم فعلى الصحيح المفتى به تخرج للوالدين في كل جمعة بإذنه وبغير إذنه، ولزيارة المحارم في كل سنة مرة بإذنه وبغير إذنه ، وأما الخروج للأهل زائداً على ذلك فلها ذلك بإذنه . قال في الظهيرية ويجوز للرجل أن يأذن لها في الخروج إلى زيارة الوالدين وتعزيتهما وعيادتهما وزيارة المحارم. وفي الخلاصة معزياً إلى مجموع النوازل: يجوز للرجل أن يأذن لها بالخروج إلى سبعة مواضع: زيارة الأبوين وعيادتهما وتعزيتهما أو أحدهما وزيارة المحارم، فإن كانت قابلة أو غسالة أو كان لها على آخر حق تخرج بالإذن وبغير الإذن والحج على هذا، وفيما عدا ذلك من زيارة الأجانب وعيادتهم والوليمة لا يأذن لها ولا تخرج، ولو أذن وخرجت كانا عاصيين. وتمنع من الحمام فإن أرادت أن تخرج إلى مجلس العلم بغير رضا الزوج ليس لها ذلك، فإن وقعت لها نازلة إن سأل الزوج من العالم أو أخبرها بذلك لا يسعها الخروج، وإن امتنع من السؤال يسعها الخروج من غير رضا الزوج، وإن لم تقع لها نازلة لكن أرادت أن تخرج إلى مجلس العلم لتتعلم مسألة من مسائل الوضوء والصلاة، فإن كان الزوج يحفظ المسائل ويذكر عندها فله أن يمنعها وإن كان لا يحفظ فالأولى أن يأذن لها أحياناً، وإن لم يأذن فلا شيء عليه، ولا يسعها الخروج ما لم يقع لها نازلة. وفي الفتاوى في باب المهر : والمرأة قبل أن تقبض مهرها لها الخروج في حوائجها وتزور الأقارب بغير إذن الزوج، فإن أعطاها المهر ليس لها الخروج إلا بإذن الزوج ا هـ . وهكذا في الخانية إلا أنه زاد أنها تخرج بغير الإذن أيضاً إذا كانت في منزل يخاف السقوط عليها. وقيد الحج بالفرض مع وجود المحرم، وقيد خروج القابلة والغاسلة بإذن الزوج، وفسر الغاسلة بمن تغسل الموتى، وينبغي أن للزوج أن يمنع القابلة والغاسلة من الخروج لأن في الخروج إضراراً به وهي وحقه مقدم على فرض الكفاية بخلاف الحج الفرض لأن حقه لا يقدم على فرض العين وينبغي أن يحمل كلامهم هنا على المرأة التي لم تكن مخدرة في مسألة خروجها للخصومة عند القاضي لأنه حينئذ لا يقبل منها التوكيل، وأما إذا كانت مخدرة فليس لها الخروج بغير محبوسة لحقه.
His statement: (And even if it is a mother or a child.) Al-Ramli said: I say: If she has a child from another husband and wants to breastfeed and raise him, can he prevent her? What must be said is that he can prevent her, as evidenced by what is in al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Kafi regarding hiring a wet nurse: The husband may prevent his wife from what causes a defect in his right. And also from it transmitting from al-Sighnaqi: Because breastfeeding and staying up tire her, and that reduces her beauty, and her beauty is the husband's right, so he may prevent her. Contemplate. End quote. And if her father is disabled, for example, and needs her service, and the husband prevents her from attending to him, then she must disobey him, whether the father is Muslim or non-Muslim; as in Fath al-Qadir. And it has been derived from what we have mentioned that she may go out to visit parents and maharim. According to the correct view on which fatwa is given, she goes out to parents every Friday with or without his permission, and to maharim once every year with or without his permission. As for going out to relatives beyond that, she may do so with his permission. It is said in al-Zahiriyyah: It is permissible for a man to permit her to go out to visit parents, offer condolences, visit the sick, and visit maharim. And in al-Khulasah attributed to Majmu' al-Nawazil: It is permissible for a man to permit her to go out to seven places: visiting parents, visiting them when sick, offering condolences to them or one of them, and visiting maharim. If she is a midwife or a corpse-washer, or she has a right over someone, she goes out with or without permission, and Hajj is likewise. Other than that, such as visiting strangers, visiting them when sick, and weddings, he does not permit her, and she does not go out. If he permits and she goes out, both are sinning. She is prevented from going to the bathhouse. If she wants to go to a knowledge gathering without the husband's consent, she may not. If a matter of inquiry occurs for her, if the husband asks the scholar or informs her, she may not go out. If he refrains from asking, she may go out without his consent. If no matter occurs but she wants to attend a gathering to learn a mas'alah of wudu or prayer, then if the husband knows the mas'ail and mentions them to her, he may prevent her; if he does not know, it is better to permit her occasionally. If he does not permit, nothing is upon him, and she may not go out unless a matter occurs. And in al-Fatawa in the chapter of mahr: A woman before receiving her mahr may go out for her needs and visit relatives without the husband's permission. If he gives her the mahr, she may not go out without his permission. End quote. Similarly in al-Khaniyyah, except it adds that she also goes out without permission if she is in a house that she fears will collapse on her. And Hajj is restricted to obligatory Hajj with a mahram. And the exit of the midwife and washer is restricted to with permission, and the washer is interpreted as one who washes the dead. It should be that the husband can prevent the midwife and washer from going out because going out harms him, and his right is prioritized over fard kifayah, unlike obligatory Hajj because his right is not prioritized over fard 'ayn. And their speech here should be understood as applying to a woman who is not secluded (mukhaddarah) in the issue of her going out for litigation before the judge, because then accepting a representative from her is not accepted. As for if she is secluded, she may not go out without being confined due to his right.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed set of rulings from various Hanafi sources about when a wife can go out of her home. It begins with the husband's right to prevent her from breastfeeding a child from a previous marriage, as it harms his right to her beauty and rest. Then it discusses caring for her own father: she must disobey her husband if he prevents her from serving her disabled father. Then it summarizes the standard rulings: she may visit parents weekly, maharim yearly, without permission. For additional visits, she needs permission. Then it lists seven specific places for which the husband should permit her: visiting parents, offering condolences, visiting the sick, and visiting maharim. Additionally, midwives and corpse-washers may go out without permission for their work. Also, she may go without permission to claim a right, and for obligatory Hajj with mahram. Going to the bathhouse is forbidden. Attending knowledge gatherings is limited: if she has a specific need (a nazilah), she may go without permission if the husband refuses to ask; otherwise, she may not. The mahr issue: before receiving mahr, she can go out without permission. The passage ends with comments on seclusion and litigation.

Key Terms
المخدرة (al-mukhaddarah): A secluded woman who does not normally go out in public.
القابلة (al-qabilah): Midwife
الغاسلة (al-ghasilah): Corpse-washer (a woman who washes the deceased).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Going to the bathhouse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives wives the right to visit immediate family regularly, but visiting non-mahram is prohibited even with the husband's permission.

Conditions for Ordering Maintenance from Absent Husband's Property
Explains conditions for judge to order maintenance from absent husband's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for a judge to order maintenance from an absent husband's property and identify which dependents are covered.

Core Idea
Maintenance for wife, child, and parents from absent husband's property requires acknowledgment of both property and the relationship.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq expands on a line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding maintenance when the husband is absent. It clarifies that the judge can only order maintenance from property held by a third party if that party acknowledges both the existence of the property and the marriage (or lineage). The commentary also explains why other relatives like brothers are excluded and critiques the matn's wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Context
The judge imposes maintenance for the wife, minor child, and parents of an absent husband from property that is present with someone else. First, note the two scenarios: property present with another, or not. The matn addresses the first.
2
List the Two Conditions for the Judge's Order
For the judge to act, the person holding the property must (1) acknowledge the property itself, and (2) acknowledge the marriage (for the wife) or lineage (for child/parents). This acknowledgment proves the right.
The acknowledgment is accepted regarding his own right, especially here.
3
Distinguish the Categories of Dependents
Only wife, minor child, and parents are covered. Why? Because their maintenance is not subject to ijtihad; it is wajib. For other relatives (e.g., brother, uncle), maintenance is subject to ijtihad and requires a court order against the absent person, which is impermissible.
Major disabled child and female child are like the minor.
4
Analyze the Textual Precision on 'cinda'
The matn uses 'cinda' (with him) which covers trustees like a depositary or mudarib. It does not cover a debtor. The commentator suggests 'cinda aw calayhi' (with him or upon him) would include debts, but combining literal and metaphorical in one word is not permissible in Arabic usul.
This reflects the Hanafi concern for precise legal language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وفرض لزوجة الغائب وطفله وأبويه في مال له عند من يقربه وبالزوجية ويؤخذ منها كفيل بيان لنفقة الزوجة إذا كان زوجها غائباً ولم يعطها نفقتها واستتبع نفقة الفروع والأصول عند غيبته، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون له مال حاضر عند غيره أو لا، فصرح بالأول وأشار إلى الثاني. أما الأول فشرط لفرض القاضي شيئين : أن يكون من عنده المال مقراً به، وأن يكون مقراً بالزوجية لأنه لما أقر بهما فقد أقر بأن حق الأخذ لها لأن لها أن من مال الزوج حقها من غير رضاه وإقرار صاحب اليد مقبول في حق نفسه لا سيما ها هنا، وكذا الولد الصغير والأبوان لأن لهم أن يأخذوا نفقتهم من ماله بغير قضاء ولا رضا وكان القضاء في حقهم إعانة وفتوى من القاضي وحكم الولد الكبير الزمن أو الأنثى مطلقاً كالصغير لما سيأتي وقيد بالطفل والأبوين للاحتراز عن غيرهم من الأقرباء كالأخ والعم فإن نفقتهم إنما تجب بالقضاء لأنه مجتهد فيه والقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، وللاحتراز عن نفقة مملوكة وأطلقه فيمن عنده المال فشمل مودعه ومضاربه قالوا وكذا مديونه، فلو قال المصنف عنده أو عليه لكان أولى لأن عنده للأمانة فلو استعملت هنا للأمانة والدين لكان جمعاً بين الحقيقة والمجاز بلفظ واحد وهو لا يجوز. وقوله «بالزوجية» اكتفاء وإلا فكان ينبغي أن يقول بالزوجية والنسب لأنه لا تفرض النفقة لطفله وأبويه حتى يقر بالنسب.
His saying: (And he [the judge] imposes maintenance for the wife of the absent [husband] and his child and his parents from his property with one who is near him [i.e., a trustee], and [acknowledgment] of marriage, and a guarantor is taken from her.) Explanation: This is an explanation of the maintenance of the wife when her husband is absent and he has not given her maintenance, and it includes the maintenance of ascendants and descendants during his absence. It is either that he has present property with someone else or not. He explicitly stated the first and alluded to the second. As for the first, the condition for the judge's imposition is two things: that the one who has the property acknowledges it, and that he acknowledges the marriage, because when he acknowledges both, he has acknowledged that her right is that she may take from the husband's property without his consent, and the acknowledgment of the possessor is accepted regarding his own right, especially here. Similarly, the minor child and the parents, because they have the right to take their maintenance from his property without a court order or consent, and the court order for them is assistance and a fatwa from the judge. The ruling for a major disabled child or a female child absolutely is like the minor, as will come. He restricted [the text] to child and parents to exclude other relatives like brother and uncle, because their maintenance is only obligatory by court order since it is subject to ijtihad, and a court order against an absent person is not permissible. Also to exclude maintenance of owned property. He used the word 'with one who is near him' generically, so it includes a depositary and a mudarib. They said: similarly, his debtor. So if the author had said 'with him or upon him' it would have been better, because 'with him' is for trusts; if used here for both trust and debt, it would combine literal and metaphorical usage in one word, which is not permissible. And his saying 'by marriage' is sufficient; otherwise he should have said 'by marriage and lineage' because maintenance for his child and parents is not imposed until he acknowledges lineage.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on the matn line 'وفرض لزوجة الغائب وطفله وأبويه في مال له عند من يقربه وبالزوجية ويؤخذ منها كفيل'. It explains the ruling on maintenance for the wife of an absent husband and his dependents (children and parents) from property held by a trustee or agent. The key conditions for the judge to order maintenance are that the person holding the property acknowledges both the property and the marriage. The text distinguishes between the wife's right (which she can take without court order) and the child/parents' right (court order is merely assistive). It also notes that for maintenance of other relatives (like brother, uncle), a court order is required, and since the absent husband cannot be summoned, no order is given. The author comments on the wording of the matn: 'عنده' (with him) covers trusts like deposit and mudaraba, but not debts; it would have been better to say 'عنده أو عليه' (with him or upon him) to include debts. Also, the matn says 'بالزوجية' (by marriage) but for children and parents, lineage acknowledgment is also needed.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-ghaa'ib): A husband who is absent (not present in the location), not necessarily traveling.
المقر (al-muqirr): The person who acknowledges (i.e., the holder of the property acknowledging the marriage and property).
المودع (al-mūdi'): Depositary; one with whom property is deposited for safekeeping (wadi'ah).
المضارب (al-mudārib): Partner in a mudarabah contract who manages the capital.
المديون (al-madyūn): Debtor; one who owes money to the absent husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what are the two conditions for the judge to impose maintenance for the absent husband's wife from property held by a third party?
A) The wife's need and the husband's absence
B) Acknowledgment of property and acknowledgment of marriage
C) A court order and a guarantor
D) The child's minority and the parents' poverty

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judge can order maintenance from an absent husband's property only when the holder acknowledges both the property and the relationship (marriage or lineage), and only for the wife, minor children, and parents.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Maintenance from the Property of an Absent Husband
Expansion of conditions for maintenance from absent's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for a judge to prescribe maintenance from an absent husband's property held by a custodian, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on selling the absent's property.

Core Idea
The custodian must acknowledge both the property and the relationship; sale of the absent's property is prohibited by agreement.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the mechanism for obtaining maintenance when the husband is absent. The commentator details the procedural conditions that must be fulfilled before a judge can order payment from the absent's property in the hands of a depositary, debtor, or mudarib. A key point is the restricted scope: only the wife, young child, and parents benefit from this facility, and the custodian's acknowledgment is essential. The passage also addresses the limitation that the property cannot be sold if it is of a different type, citing an intra-Hanafi agreement on the prohibition of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core conditions
The text states that for the judge to prescribe maintenance, two conditions are required: the custodian's acknowledgment of the property belonging to the absent, and acknowledgment of the relationship (marriage for wife, lineage for child/parents).
The custodian's acknowledgment is accepted regarding his own self, especially here.
2
Understand the scope of dependents
The passage notes that only wife, young child, and parents are included; other relatives (brother, uncle) are excluded because their maintenance requires a decree on the absent, which is not allowed.
Adult disabled child or daughter is treated like a young child.
3
Examine the property type restriction
Maintenance can only be prescribed from property of the same type (e.g., cash for cash, food for food). If different, sale is needed but sale of the absent's property is prohibited.
If the property is of a different type, the judge cannot order sale because it is not permissible.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on sale
Abu Hanifa prohibits sale by analogy with a present person; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prohibit it because the absent's refusal is unknown, but both agree on prohibition.
The text says 'لا يباع مال الغائب بالاتفاق' – it is agreed that the absent's property is not sold.
5
Consider the surety requirement
The judge takes a surety from the wife to cover contingencies like prior payment or her being recalcitrant.
This protects the husband's rights.

كان خصماً؛ كذا في الخانية من كتاب الوديعة وهي مما يستثنى من قولهم كل من أقر بشيء لزمه فإذا أنكره يحلف عليه، ولم يذكر المصنف استحلاف المرأة قبل الفرض. وفي الذخيرة: فإن القاضي يسأل المرأة هل عجل لها النفقة فإن قالت لا يستحلفها فإذا حلفت أمرهما القاضي بإعطاء النفقة من ذلك. وفي الخانية أنه يحلفها أنه ما أعطاها نفقة ولا كانت ناشزة وقيد بنفقة من ذكر للاحتراز عن دين على الغائب فإن صاحب الدين لو أحضر عدتها فكان النظر له في التكفيل بخلاف أخذ الكفيل
ثم قال: وفي فتح القدير: وحيث أبحنا لها الخروج فإنما يباح بشرط عدم الزينة وتغيير الهيئة إلى ما لا يكون داعية لنظر الرجال والاستمالة قال الله تعالى ولا تبرجن تبرج الجاهلية الأولى [الأحزاب: ٣٣] وقول الفقيه وتمنع من الحمام خالفه قاضيخان قال في فصل الحمام في فتاواه حيث قال دخول الحمام مشروع للرجال والنساء جميعاً خلافاً لما قاله بعض الناس إلى آخره .
قال في الشرنبلالية : يمكن أن يقال إنه لا مخالفة لأن المشروعية لا تنافي المنع ألا ترى يمنعها من صوم النفل وإن كان مشروعاً .اهـ. قوله : (إلى آخره) تمامه كما في الفتح روي أن رسول الله ﷺ دخل الحمام وتنور وخالد بن الوليد دخل حمام حمص. لكن إنما يباح إذا لم يكن فيه إنسان مكشوف العورة .اهـ. قال في الفتح : وعلى ذلك فلا خلاف في منعهن من دخوله للعلم بأن كثيراً منهن مكشوف العورة وقد وردت أحاديث عن رسول الله ﷺ تؤيد قول الفقيه بمنعهن من دخوله وساقها قال وورد استثناء النفساء والمريضة رواه أبو داود وابن ماجه عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما
ثم عاد إلى شرح النفقة فقال: ولا يمين للمرأة عليه الخ فلو قال وفيته هل لها عليه يمين الظاهر لا لأنها ليست خصماً في ذلك تأمل. وفي المقدسي: فلو ادعى طلاقها ومضى عدتها وله بينة ينبغي أن لا تقبل في حق الطلاق بل في منع ما تحت يده، وكذا لو قال المودع ونحوه لنا بينة إن زوجها دفع لها نفقة تكفيها قبل غيبته ينبغي قبولها .
قوله : (وفي بعض النسخ ويؤخذ منه يؤيد هذه النسخة ما في التتارخانية : للقاضي أن يعطي
And with her, and he specified for the absent wife, his child, and his parents in his wealth with someone close to him, and by marriage, and a surety is taken from her. Explanation of the maintenance of the wife when her husband is absent and has not given her her maintenance, and it includes the maintenance of branches and roots during his absence. It is either that he has present wealth with someone else or not. He stated the first and alluded to the second. As for the first, the condition for the judge to prescribe is two things: that the one with the wealth acknowledges it, and that he acknowledges the marriage, because when he acknowledges both, he has acknowledged that the right to take is hers, because she has from her husband's wealth her right without his consent, and the acknowledgment of the possessor is accepted regarding his own self, especially here. Likewise the young child and the parents, because they can take their maintenance from his wealth without a decree or consent, and the decree for them is assistance and a fatwa from the judge. And the ruling of the adult disabled son or daughter absolutely is like the young child as will come. He restricted to the child and parents to exclude other relatives like brother and uncle, for their maintenance is only obligatory by decree because it is subject to ijtihad, and decree upon the absent is not permissible. And to exclude the maintenance of a slave. He gave it absolutely regarding who has the wealth, so it includes his depositary and his mudarib. They said: likewise his debtor. If the author said 'with him' or 'upon him', it would be better because 'with him' is for trust, so if used here for trust and debt, it would be combining literal and figurative in one word, which is not permissible. His saying 'by marriage' is sufficient, otherwise he should have said by marriage and lineage, because maintenance is not prescribed for his child and parents until lineage is acknowledged as in al-Tabyin. They said: the judge's knowledge of both is like his acknowledgment of both. If the judge knows one, he needs acknowledgment of the other according to the correct view. He gave it absolutely regarding wealth, and it is in need of restriction. They said: this is when the wealth is of the same type as her right—dirhams, dinars, gold, food, or clothing of the same type as her right. If it is of a different type than her right, maintenance is not prescribed for it because it requires sale, and the property of the absent is not sold by agreement. According to Abu Hanifa, because it is not sold on the present, so likewise on the absent. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because it is decreed on the present because his refusal is known; it is not decreed on the absent because his refusal is not known. He restricted by his acknowledgment of both, because if he denies that the wealth is for the absent, or denies the marriage, or denies both, the woman's evidence is not accepted for any of that. Regarding the wealth, because by this evidence she establishes ownership for the absent, and she is not a litigant in establishing ownership for the absent. Regarding the marriage, because by this evidence she establishes the marriage on the absent, and the depositary and debtor are not litigants in establishing marriage on the absent. And there is no oath for the woman upon him because only a litigant is sworn. Thus in al-Khaniyya from the book of deposits, and it is an exception from their statement: whoever acknowledges something, it is binding on him; if he denies it, he is sworn upon it. And the author did not mention the swearing of the woman before the prescription. In al-Dhakhira: the judge asks the woman if he has advanced the maintenance to her. If she says no, he swears her. When she swears, the judge orders them to give the maintenance from that. In al-Khaniyya, he swears her that he has not given her maintenance and that she has not been recalcitrant. He restricted by the maintenance of those mentioned to exclude a debt owed by the absent. For the creditor, if he brings a debtor or depositary of the absent, the judge does not order repayment of the debt, even if he acknowledges the wealth and the debt, because the judge only orders regarding the absent what is a benefit to him and preservation of his property. Spending on his wife from his property is preservation of his property, while paying his debt is a decree against him based on the word of another, which is not permissible. Thus in al-Dhakhira. And he gave absolutely in prescribing maintenance, so it includes when the depositary says: the husband ordered me not to give her anything. The judge does not pay attention to that and orders him to spend, and there is no liability upon him. Thus in al-Dhakhira. The pronoun in the author's saying 'he prescribed' refers to what was mentioned first, i.e., the three: prescribing maintenance, clothing, and lodging as in al-Dhakhira. And he only takes a surety from her because it is possible that he has advanced her the maintenance, or she was recalcitrant, or she is a divorced woman whose idda has expired, so it is in his interest to take a surety, unlike taking a surety from the other party.
Then he said: In Fath al-Qadir: and where we permit her to go out, it is only permitted on condition of no adornment and changing her appearance to something that does not invite men's looks and attraction. Allah says: 'And do not display yourself as in the first Jahiliyyah' (33:33). And the jurist's statement: and she is prohibited from the bathhouse. Qadikhan disagreed. He said in the chapter on the bathhouse in his fatwas: entering the bathhouse is permissible for both men and women, contrary to what some people say, etc.
He said in al-Sharnablaliyya: It can be said that there is no contradiction because permissibility does not negate prohibition. Do you not see that he prevents her from supererogatory fasting even though it is permissible? etc. His saying: (etc.) Its completion as in al-Fath: It is narrated that the Messenger of Allah entered the bathhouse and oven, and Khalid ibn al-Walid entered the bathhouse of Hims. But it is only permissible if there is no one with exposed 'awra. etc. He said in al-Fath: on that basis, there is no difference in prohibiting them from entering, because it is known that many of them have exposed 'awra, and hadiths from the Messenger of Allah have been narrated supporting the jurist's statement prohibiting them from entering, and he listed them. He said: and the exception of the postpartum woman and the sick woman is reported by Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah from Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them.
Then he returned to explaining maintenance and said: and there is no oath for the woman upon him, etc. If he says 'and I paid it' does she have an oath? Apparently no, because she is not a litigant in that. Consider. In al-Maqdisi: if he claims her divorce and the expiry of her idda, and he has evidence, it should not be accepted regarding the divorce but regarding preventing what is in his hand. Likewise if the depositary or similar says: we have evidence that her husband gave her sufficient maintenance before his absence, it should be accepted.
His saying: (and in some copies 'and it is taken from him' supports this copy what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya: the judge can give...

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling on maintenance (nafaqa) for the wife, child, and parents of an absent husband. The commentator explains that if the absent husband has property held by a custodian (such as a depositary or debtor), the judge can order maintenance from that property under two conditions: the custodian must acknowledge both that the property belongs to the absent person and that the relationship (marriage or lineage) exists. The text emphasizes that the custodian's acknowledgment is sufficient for the judge to act, even without the absent person's consent. For the child and parents, acknowledgment of lineage is also required. The type of property must match the type of maintenance (e.g., dirhams for cash, food for food); otherwise, sale would be needed, which is generally prohibited for an absent person's property. Here, an intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Hanifa prohibits sale by analogy with a present person, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prohibit it because the absent's refusal is unknown. The passage then discusses the taking of a surety from the wife to cover the possibility that maintenance was already given. A separate sub-discussion addresses the wife's exit from the house: she may leave only without adornment, citing Quran 33:33, and the text reports a dispute about women entering the bathhouse. The commentator reconciles the prohibition with Qadikhan's permissibility by noting that permissibility does not preclude prohibition in specific circumstances. The passage concludes with additional notes on oaths and evidence in maintenance disputes.

Key Terms
الزوجية (al-zawjiyya): Marital relationship; the condition that the custodian must acknowledge the marriage between the absent husband and the woman claiming maintenance.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage; for children and parents, the custodian must acknowledge their blood relationship to the absent person to entitle them to maintenance.
المودع (al-mudi'): Depositary; a person with whom the absent person's property is deposited, who is among those from whom maintenance can be ordered.
الكفيل (al-kafil): Surety; a guarantor that the wife provides when taking maintenance in advance, to protect the husband's rights if it turns out she was not entitled.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): Absent person; the husband who is away and has not provided maintenance, whose property is being used to support his dependents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the judge to prescribe maintenance from an absent husband's property?
A) The custodian acknowledges the property belongs to the husband.
B) The custodian acknowledges the marriage or lineage.
C) The wife provides two male witnesses to the marriage.
D) The property is of the same type as the maintenance claimed.

Key Takeaway
The judge's prescription of maintenance for dependents of an absent husband requires the custodian's acknowledgment of both the property and the relationship; sale of property of a different type is prohibited by agreement among Hanafis.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Nafaqah from Deposited Property of an Absent Husband
Ruling on nafaqah from deposited property of absent husband with conditions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may order nafaqah from deposited property of an absent husband and explain the Hanafi reasoning for the restriction on selling absent property.

Core Idea
Nafaqah from absent husband's deposit requires his acknowledgment and same-type property; selling absent property is prohibited by consensus.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the conditions for enforcing nafaqah from property an absent husband has left in deposit. It explains the procedural prerequisites—acknowledgment of marriage and ownership—and the critical substantive limitation: the property must match the wife's right in type. The underlying rationale is the absolute prohibition (by Hanafi consensus) on selling the property of an absent person, which the author substantiates with two distinct lines of reasoning from Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? It is the judge's order to pay nafaqah from deposited property of an absent husband. The ruling is that this is permissible only under specific conditions.
The act is the judge's enforcement, not the husband's obligation per se.
2
Locate the conditions (shurut)
The passage lists conditions: (1) the husband must have acknowledged both the marriage and that the deposited property belongs to him; (2) the property must be of the same type as the wife's right (dirhams, dinars, food, clothing); (3) the wife must swear that she has not received nafaqah and is not nashizah.
If the judge knows one condition but not the other, he still needs the missing acknowledgment.
3
Trace the dalil and its reasoning
The central proof is the consensus (ittifaq) that the property of an absent person cannot be sold. The text gives two lines of reasoning: Abu Hanifah's and that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. These are both justifications for the consensus.
The reasoning is talil (rationally derived) rather than directly from a text.
4
Examine the intra-madhhab difference in 'illah
Abu Hanifah argues from a stronger-to-weaker (awlawi) analogy: if selling is not allowed against a present person (who can object), it is even more so against an absent one. The Sahibayn argue that selling is allowed against a present person because his refusal is known; it is prohibited against an absent person because his refusal is unknown.
Both reach the same ruling but differ in the effective cause (illah).
5
Note the procedural safeguard (istihlaf)
Before ordering payment, the judge must ask the wife to swear that she has not received nafaqah and was not nashizah. This protects the absent husband's interest.
This is an exception to the general rule that a person who acknowledges a claim is bound by it; here the denial triggers a sworn statement.

Source Text

يكون مقراً بالنسب كما في التبيين قالوا: وعلم القاضي بهما كإقراره بهما، وإن علم القاضي أحدهما يحتاج إلى الإقرار بالآخر على الصحيح وأطلق في المال وهو في محل التقييد قالوا: هذا إذا كان المال من جنس حقها دراهم أو دنانير أو تبراً أو طعاماً أو كسوة من جنس حقها، أما إذا كان من خلاف جنس حقها لا تفرض النفقة فيه لأنه يحتاج إلى البيع ولا يباع مال الغائب بالاتفاق أما عند أبي حنيفة فلأنه لا يباع على الحاضر فكذا على الغائب، وأما عندهما فلأنه إن كان يقضى على الحاضر لأنه يعرف امتناعه لا يقضى على الغائب، لأنه لا يعرف امتناعه وقيد بإقراره بهما لأنه لو جحد كون المال للغائب أو جحد النكاح أو جحدهما لم تقبل بينهما على شيء من ذلك، أما على المال فلأنها بهذه البينة تثبت الملك للغائب وهي ليست بخصم في إثبات الملك للغائب وأما على الزوجية فلأنها بهذه البينة تثبت النكاح على الغائب والمودع والمديون ليسا بخصم في إثبات النكاح على الغائب ولا يمين للمرأة عليه لأنه لا يستحلف إلا من كان خصماً؛ كذا في الخانية من كتاب الوديعة وهي مما يستثنى من قولهم كل من أقر بشيء لزمه فإذا أنكره يحلف عليه، ولم يذكر المصنف استحلاف المرأة قبل الفرض.

وفي الذخيرة: فإن القاضي يسأل المرأة هل عجل لها النفقة فإن قالت لا يستحلفها فإذا حلفت أمرهما القاضي بإعطاء النفقة من ذلك. وفي الخانية أنه يحلفها أنه ما أعطاها نفقة ولا كانت ناشزة وقيد بنفقة من ذكر للاحتراز عن دين على الغائب فإن صاحب الدين لو أحضر غريماً أو مودعاً للغائب لم يأمره القاضي بقضاء الدين وإن كان مقراً بالمال وبدينه لأن القاضي إنما يأمر في حق الغائب بما يكون نظراً له وحفظاً لملكه، وفي الإنفاق على زوجته من ماله حفظ ملكه وفي وفاء دينه قضاء عليه بقول الغير وهو لا يجوز؛ كذا في الذخيرة. وأطلق في فرض النفقة فشمل ما إذا قال المودع إن الزوج أمرني أن لا أدفع إليها شيئاً فإن القاضي لا يلتفت إليه ويأمره بالإنفاق ولا ضمان عليه؛ كذا في الذخيرة.
He is considered to have acknowledged lineage as in al-Tabyin. They said: The judge's knowledge of both (marriage and property) is like his acknowledgment of both. If the judge knows one of them, he needs acknowledgment of the other according to the correct position. And he (the judge) was absolute regarding the property, but it is in a place of restriction. They said: This is when the property is of the same type as her right – dirhams or dinars or bullion or food or clothing of the same type as her right. But if it is of a different type from her right, nafaqah is not assessed in it because it requires sale, and the property of an absent person cannot be sold by consensus. According to Abu Hanifah, because it is not sold against a present person, so likewise against an absent person. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because if it is ruled against a present person because his refusal is known, it is not ruled against an absent person because his refusal is not known. And it is restricted by his acknowledgment of both because if he denies that the property belongs to the absent person, or denies the marriage, or denies both, her evidence is not accepted for any of that. As for the property, because by this evidence she proves ownership for the absent person, and she is not a litigant in proving ownership for the absent person. As for the marital relationship, because by this evidence she proves the marriage against the absent person, and the depositary and the debtor are not litigants in proving the marriage against the absent person. And the woman has no oath against him because no one is sworn except a litigant; thus in al-Khaniyah from Kitab al-Wadi'ah. And this is an exception to their statement: 'Whoever acknowledges something, it becomes binding on him; if he denies it, he is sworn upon it.' And the author did not mention the woman's swearing before assessment. In al-Dhakhirah: The judge asks the woman if he paid her nafaqah. If she says no, he makes her swear. When she swears, the judge orders them (the depositary and debtor) to give her nafaqah from that. And in al-Khaniyah: He makes her swear that he did not give her nafaqah and that she was not nashizah (rebellious). And it is restricted to the nafaqah of those mentioned to exclude a debt against the absent person. For if the creditor brings a debtor or depositary of the absent person, the judge does not order him to pay the debt, even if he acknowledges the property and the debt, because the judge only orders for the absent person what is in his interest and preservation of his property. Spending on his wife from his property preserves his property, but paying his debt is a ruling against him based on another's statement, which is not permissible; thus in al-Dhakhirah. And (the term) assessment (farad) of nafaqah is absolute, so it includes the case where the depositary says: 'The husband ordered me not to give her anything.' The judge does not pay attention to him and orders him to spend, and there is no liability on him; thus in al-Dhakhirah.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary (sharh) of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, expanding on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding nafaqah for an absent husband when there is deposited property. The judge can order nafaqah from that property only if the husband has acknowledged both the marriage and that the property is his. If the judge knows one, he needs the other. The property must be of the same type as the wife's right (e.g., dirhams for cash nafaqah, food for food). If it is of a different type, nafaqah is not assessed because it would require selling the absent person's property, which is prohibited by consensus of the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifah's reasoning is that if selling is not allowed against a present person due to the possibility of objection, it is even more so for an absent person. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say that selling is allowed against a present person because his refusal is known, but against an absent person it is not allowed because his refusal is unknown. The wife's evidence is not accepted if the depositary denies the marriage or ownership, because she is not a litigant in proving ownership for the absent husband. The judge also makes her swear that she has not received nafaqah and that she is not nashizah. This ruling is specific to nafaqah, not debts; the judge will not order payment of a debt owed by the absent person because that would be a ruling against him based on another's claim, which is not allowed. However, if the depositary claims the husband told him not to pay, the judge ignores that and orders payment anyway, with no liability on the depositary.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment by the husband of the marriage and that the deposited property belongs to him; required for the judge to act.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof brought by the wife, but not accepted here to prove ownership or marriage because she is not a litigant in those matters.
خصم (khaṣm): Litigant; a person who is a party to a dispute and can be sworn or adjudicated against. The wife is not a litigant in proving ownership or marriage for the absent husband.
غائب (ghā'ib): Absent person, usually the husband who is away and has left property in deposit.
وديعة (wadī‘ah): Deposit; property left with a person for safekeeping. Here, the deposited property may be used for nafaqah under conditions.
استحلاف (istiḥlāf): Swearing of the wife by the judge to confirm she has not received nafaqah and is not nashizah before ordering payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be satisfied regarding the property of the absent husband for the judge to order nafaqah from it?
A) The property must be of any type as long as it is valuable.
B) The property must be of the same type as the wife's right (e.g., dirhams for cash nafaqah).
C) The property must be acknowledged by the depositary as belonging to the husband.
D) The wife must first prove that the husband explicitly ordered the deposit to be used for her nafaqah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes strict procedural and substantive conditions for accessing an absent husband's deposited property for nafaqah, emphasizing preservation of his property rights through the bar on sale.

Guarantor and Nafaqah for Absent Husband
Rulings on taking kafil and procedure when husband has no property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a kafil from the wife and explain the procedure when the absent husband has no property, including the role of evidence and borrowing.

Core Idea
A judge may take a kafil as precaution; if husband has no property, wife borrows and must later re-prove if denied.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains the procedural safeguards when a wife seeks nafaqah from an absent husband. The judges of the author's time had adopted a more lenient practice, justified by scholarly disagreement. You will learn both the authoritative rulings of the three imams and the accepted practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the precautionary rationale for taking a kafil
The judge may require a guarantor from the wife to protect the absent husband's interest, as there may be hidden reasons he already paid, she may be nashizah, or her iddah may have expired.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether taking a kafil is wajib or hasan
Sarakhsi says wajib, Khassaf says hasan. Al-Sadr al-Shahid prefers wajib, but Kitab al-Aqdiya indicates it is optional precaution.
3
Apply the rule when the husband has property present
If the husband has property at home, the judge assesses nafaqah directly from that property without ruling against the husband, analogous to the hadith of Hind.
4
Examine the procedure when the husband has no property
The wife provides evidence, the judge orders her to borrow on the husband's account; if husband later denies, she must re-prove or repay.
5
Analyze the justification for the judges' lenient practice
The practice of accepting the wife's evidence without husband's acknowledgment is upheld due to ikhtilaf and public convenience.
The text notes this is not the zahir al-riwayah view but is valid because it is mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضمير في قول المصنف «فرض» يعود إلى ما ذكر أولاً وهو الثلاثة أي فرض النفقة والكسوة والسكني كما في الذخيرة وإنما يأخذ منها كفيلاً لجواز أنه قد عجل لها النفقة أو كانت ناشزة أو مطلقة قد انقضت عدتها فكان النظر له في التكفيل بخلاف أخذ الكفيل عند قسمة التركة بين الورثة فإنه ليس بحسن لجهالة المكفول له كما سيأتي. واختلف في أخذ الكفيل هل هو واجب على القاضي أو حسن؟ ذهب السرخسي إلى الأول والخصاف إلى الثاني، وصحح الصدر الشهيد الأول لأنه نصب ناظراً للعاجز فيجب عليه النظر إليه وهو في أخذ الكفيل. وفي كتاب الأقضية أن القاضي لو لم يأخذ منها كفيلاً دفع إليها النفقة، فهذا إشارة إلى أن أخذ الكفيل نوع احتياط لا أن يكون لازماً؛ كذا في الذخيرة. وذكر في المستصفى قوله ويؤخذ منها أي من المرأة وفي بعض النسخ، ويؤخذ منه أي من آخذ النفقة أو من كل واحد من الأصناف المذكورين اهـ. وهذا يدل على أنه يؤخذ الكفيل من الوالدين أيضاً وهو الظاهر لأنه أنظر للغائب. وقد يقال إنه إنما يؤخذ منها لما تقدم، وأما من الوالدين فإنما هو لاحتمال التعجيل، وقدمنا أن النفقة المعجلة للقريب إذا هلكت أو سرقت فإنه يقضى له بأخرى بخلاف الزوجة فليس في أخذ الكفيل احتياط للغائب لأنه لو كان عجل ثم ادعى الوالد هلاكها قبل منه . وقيد بكون المال عند شخص لأنه لو كان له مال في بيته فطلبت من القاضي فرض النفقة ؛ فإن علم بالنكاح بينهما فرض لها في ذلك المال لأنه إيفاء لحق المرأة وليس بقضاء على الزوج بالنفقة كما لو أقر بدين ثم غاب وله مال حاضر من جنس الدين وطلب صاحب الدين من ذلك قضى له به، أصله حديث هند كما عرف وينبغي للقاضي أن يحلفها أنه لم يعطها النفقة ويأخذ منها كفيلاً كما قدمناه ؛ كذا في الذخيرة ولو لم يكن له مال أصلاً فطلبت من القاضي فرض النفقة فعندنا يسمع البينة لأنه قضاء على الغائب، وعند زفر يسمع القاضي البينة ولا يقضي بالنكاح ويعطيها النفقة من مال الزوج وإن لم يكن له مال أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة، فإن حضر الزوج وأقر بالنكاح أمره بقضاء الدين وإن أنكر ذلك كلفها القاضي إعادة البينة، فإن لم تعدها أمرها القاضي برد ما أخذت وما يفعله القضاة في زماننا من قبول البينة من المرأة وفرض النفقة على الغائب إنما ينفذ لا لأنه قول علمائنا الثلاثة في ظاهر الرواية وإنما ينفذ لكونه مختلفاً فيه، إما مع زفر أو مع أبي يوسف كما ذكره الخصاف وهو أرفق بالناس. ثم على قول من يقول تفرض النفقة في هذه المسألة لا تحتاج المرأة إقامة البيئة على أنه لم يخلف نفقة؛ كذا في الذخيرة والخانية والحاصل أن القاضي إذا لم يعلم النكاح فليس له فرض النفقة على الغائب ولو أقامت المرأة البينة على ظاهر الرواية لكن لو سمع البينة وفرضها لا النفقة لهؤلاء من مال الغائب إذا استوثق بكفيل من أحد فحسن
The pronoun in the author's statement 'assessment' (farad) refers to what was mentioned first, namely the three: assessment of nafaqah, clothing, and dwelling as in al-Dhakhirah. He only takes a guarantor (kafil) from her because of the possibility that he may have already paid her nafaqah, or she may be nashizah, or a divorced woman whose iddah has expired. So taking a kafil is in the interest of the absent husband, unlike taking a kafil in the division of inheritance among heirs, which is not good due to the ignorance of the guaranteed person (majhul al-makful lahu) as will come. And there is a difference of opinion regarding taking a kafil: is it obligatory upon the judge or merely good? Sarakhsi went for the first, Khassaf for the second. Al-Sadr al-Shahid corrected the first because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for the incapable, so he must supervise, and that is in taking a kafil. In Kitab al-Aqdiya, that if the judge does not take a kafil from her, he pays her the nafaqah. This indicates that taking a kafil is a type of precaution, not binding; thus in al-Dhakhirah. And in al-Mustasfa, it is mentioned: 'He takes from her' meaning from the woman; and in some copies, 'He takes from him' meaning from the recipient of nafaqah or from each of the mentioned categories. This indicates that a kafil is taken from the parents as well, which is apparent because it is more in the interest of the absent person. It may be said that he only takes from her for the reason mentioned, but from the parents it is due to the possibility of advance payment. And we have already stated that advance nafaqah for a relative, if lost or stolen, another is judged for it, unlike the wife. So there is no precaution for the absent person in taking a kafil from the parents, because if he advanced it and then the parent claims its loss, he is believed. And it is restricted to the property being with a person because if he has property in his house and she asks the judge to assess nafaqah, then if he knows the marriage between them, he assesses it from that property because it is fulfilling the woman's right and not a ruling against the husband for nafaqah, just as if he acknowledged a debt then went absent and has present property of the same type as the debt, and the creditor claims from it, he is ruled to give it to him. Its origin is the hadith of Hind as known. And the judge should make her swear that he did not give her nafaqah and take a kafil from her as we have mentioned; thus in al-Dhakhirah. If he has no property at all and she asks the judge to assess nafaqah, then in our view he hears the evidence because it is a ruling against the absent person. According to Zufar, the judge hears the evidence but does not rule on the marriage, and gives her nafaqah from the husband's wealth. If he has no property, the judge orders her to borrow (istidanah). If the husband appears and acknowledges the marriage, he orders him to pay the debt; if he denies it, the judge requires her to re-present the evidence; if she does not, the judge orders her to return what she took. And what the judges in our time do, accepting evidence from the woman and assessing nafaqah against the absent person, is only valid not because it is the saying of our three imams in the zahir al-riwayah, but because it is a matter of disagreement, either with Zufar or with Abu Yusuf, as mentioned by al-Khassaf, and it is more convenient for the people. Then, according to those who say that nafaqah is assessed in this case, the woman does not need to produce evidence that he did not leave nafaqah; thus in al-Dhakhirah and al-Khaniyah. The upshot is that if the judge does not know the marriage, he has no right to assess nafaqah against the absent person, even if the woman brings evidence according to the zahir al-riwayah. However, if he hears the evidence and assesses it, [the nafaqah] for these [women] from the absent person's property is valid only if he secures a guarantor from someone.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on nafaqah for an absent husband. It first explains that the pronoun in 'farad' refers to three things: nafaqah, clothing, and shelter. The judge may take a kafil (guarantor) from the wife for precautionary reasons, such as the husband may have already paid, she may be nashizah, or her iddah may have expired. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Sarakhsi holds it is wajib on the judge, while Khassaf considers it merely good (hasan). Al-Sadr al-Shahid prefers Sarakhsi's view. However, Kitab al-Aqdiya indicates it is optional. The guarantor is taken from the wife and also from parents if they receive nafaqah for a relative, but for parents it is less stringent. If the husband has property at home, the judge can assess nafaqah directly from it, similar to a debt. If the husband has no property, the judge hears the wife's evidence (bayyinah) and orders her to borrow on the husband's account. If the husband later denies the marriage, she must re-prove it or return the money. The judges in our time often accept the wife's evidence and rule nafaqah without the husband's presence, which is allowed due to the difference of opinion, being more convenient. The key principle: without knowledge of the marriage, the judge cannot assess nafaqah against an absent person based on the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafīl): Guarantor; a person who guarantees that the nafaqah taken by the wife is legitimate, to protect the absent husband's interest.
احتياط (iḥtiyāṭ): Precaution; taking a kafil is a precautionary measure, not obligatory.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence; here the wife's evidence of marriage and lack of nafaqah, which the judge accepts according to some views.
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The well-known transmitted position of the Hanafi school; here it refers to the majority view of the three imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the consequence for the wife if the absent husband returns and denies the marriage after she has taken a loan based on the judge's order?
A) The loan is considered a gift and need not be repaid.
B) The husband must pay the debt regardless of denial.
C) The wife must re-present evidence of the marriage, and if she cannot, return the loan.
D) The wife is automatically entitled to the borrowed amount as nafaqah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judicial procedure for nafaqah of an absent husband balances precaution (kafil) with practical convenience, allowing the wife to borrow on his account but holding her liable if she cannot prove the marriage later.
