The Wife's Borrowing for Maintenance: The Six Issues Exception
Statutory ruling on the validity of a judge's order for borrowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a judge may order a wife to borrow for her maintenance, and explain why this ruling is one of the 'six issues' (al-masā'il al-sitt) where the Hanafi school adopts the opinion of Zufar due to necessity.

Core Idea
A judge can order a wife to borrow for maintenance – one of six exceptions where Zufar's view becomes mufta bihi.

In Hanafi fiqh, the normal rule is that the husband is responsible for the wife's maintenance. But what happens when the husband is absent and has left no funds? This lesson examines a special ruling from the later Hanafi tradition: the judge may order the wife to borrow for her own maintenance, and this borrowing is valid and enforceable. This is not just a practical solution; it is part of a known set of six issues (al-masā'il al-sitt) where the school departs from the primary position of Imam Abu Hanifa and follows the opinion of Imam Zufar due to the people's need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The passage speaks of 'ordering her to borrow' (amruhā bi al-istidānah). Identify the act: the judge's order for the wife to take a loan for her maintenance. The ruling is that it is permissible (jāiz) and legally effective (nāfidh).
The wife herself is not the subject of the ruling; the validity of the judge's order is what is being judged.
2
Attribute the position to the correct authorities
The text says this is the view of Zufar and Abu Yusuf, and that the practice ('amal) is upon it. Note that the primary Hanafi position (of Abu Hanifa) may differ; this is a departure for necessity.
Later source Fath al-Qadir reinforces this by reporting a similar transmission from Abu Yusuf.
3
Classify the ruling as one of the six exceptional issues
The passage explicitly says this is one of 'al-masā'il al-sitt' – the six issues where fatwa is given according to Zufar's view. Recognize that this is a known category in Hanafi fatwa practice, reflecting a principle of necessity (hājah).
These six issues are exceptions to the general rule that the mufta bihi is from Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn.
4
Understand the rationale of necessity
The phrase 'due to the need of the people' (li-hājat al-nās) indicates the legal cause for adopting this opinion. Necessity overrides the usual preference for the majority position.
This shows how fiqh adapts to social realities without abandoning the school's framework.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Consider a case: husband travels abroad with no communication and has left no money for the wife. The judge may order her to borrow for food and shelter. The debt becomes the husband's liability. This is the operative ruling.
The borrowing is not the wife's personal debt; it is a loan on behalf of the husband, enforced for maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمرها بالاستدانة جاز ونفذ كما هو قول زفر وأبي يوسف وعلينه العمل وهي من إحدى المسائل الست التي يفتى فيها بقول زفر لحاجة الناس. وفي فتح القدير: ونقل مثل قول زفر عن أبي يوسف فقوي عمل القضاة لحاجة الناس إلى ذلك.
And ordering her to borrow is permissible and executable, as is the view of Zufar and Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the practice, and it is one of the six issues in which fatwa is given according to Zufar's view due to the need of the people. And in Fath al-Qadir: The like of Zufar's view was transmitted from Abu Yusuf, so the practice of the judges was strengthened due to the need of the people for that.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the scenario where a wife is ordered by the judge to borrow money for her maintenance when the husband is absent and has not left any funds. The ruling states that such borrowing is valid (jāz) and effective (nāfidh). The relied-upon position (alayhi al-‘amal) is the view of Imām Zufar and Imām Abū Yūsuf. This is noteworthy because it is one of the six masā'il (issues) where the fatwā is given according to Zufar's opinion, even though the school's primary position (of Imam Abu Hanifa) may differ, due to the pressing need of the people (hājat al-nās). The commentator then quotes from Fath al-Qadīr, stating that a similar view to Zufar's was transmitted from Abū Yūsuf, which strengthened the practice of judges to act on it. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that in certain cases of necessity, a weaker opinion within the school may be adopted for fatwa and judicial practice, especially when it meets the needs of the community. The passage does not give the full proof, but the authority weight is from later fatwa practice (fatawa muta'akhkhirin) as it is a relied-upon position for judicial practice.

Key Terms
المسائل الست (al-masā'il al-sitt): The six issues in which fatwa is given according to Zufar's view due to necessity.
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Borrowing money for maintenance as ordered by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a judge to order a wife to borrow for maintenance, adopting Zufar's view as one of six necessity-based exceptions.

Mother's Duty to Spend When Father is Absent
States the mother's obligation and judge's procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a mother must spend on her young children and explain the judge's procedure for maintenance loans.

Core Idea
The mother must spend on young children if the father is absent and she has means, with a right of recourse.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a critical scenario in family maintenance: when the father disappears, leaving no support for his young children (sighār). The Hanafi school imposes an immediate obligation on the mother to spend from her own wealth, but she can reclaim it later. The text also outlines the judge's practical steps—from verifying the marriage to ordering the wife to borrow and reclaim upon the husband's return. Let's unpack these two interrelated rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage sets a specific condition: the woman has young children, the father is absent (ghāba), and he left no maintenance (lam yatruk nafaqah).
Only applies when children are young (sighār) and father is completely absent.
2
Extract the mother's ruling
The mother is compelled (tujbar) to spend on the children if she has money. This is an obligation (wājib) upon her, not optional. She then has the right of recourse (tarji‘) to reclaim the spent amount from the father when he returns.
The compulsion is by the judge (al-qāḍī), as implied by 'tujbar'.
3
Understand the judge's expanded procedure
From the mother's ruling, the commentator deduces the standard judicial process: the judge first verifies the marriage by her evidence if not already aware, then fixes maintenance (farḍ) for both wife and children, then orders her to borrow (al-istidānah).
This is the operative practice ('alayhi al-‘amal) for Hanafi judges.
4
Trace the recourse mechanism
Once the husband returns, the wife reclaims the fixed amount (al-mafrūḍ) that she spent out of pocket or borrowed. The borrowing is also reclaimable.
The judge's order to borrow creates a debt on the husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان للمرأة أولاد صغار وغاب الأب ولم يترك لهم نفقة تجبر الأم على الإنفاق إن كان لها مال ثم ترجع بذلك على الأب ؛ كذا في الخانية. وبهذا علم أن الرجل إذا غاب وله زوجة وأولاد صغار ولم يترك شيئاً فإن القاضي يسمع البينة منها على النكاح إن لم يكن عالماً به على ما عليه العمل ثم يفرض لها ولأولادها نفقة ثم يأمرها بالاستدانة، فإذا جاء رجعت عليه بالمفروض لها ولأولادها.
And if the woman has young children and the father is absent and did not leave maintenance for them, the mother is compelled to spend if she has money, then she can claim it back from the father; thus in al-Khaniya. And by this it is known that when a man is absent and he has a wife and young children and did not leave anything, then the judge hears her evidence regarding the marriage if he is not aware of it, according to the practice, then he fixes maintenance for her and her children, then orders her to borrow, and when he returns, she reclaims from him the fixed amount for her and her children.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First, from al-Khaniya: if a woman has young children (sighār) and the father is absent (ghāba al-ab) and did not leave any maintenance (nafaqah) for them, the mother is compelled (tujbar) to spend on them if she has money. She then has the right to reclaim (tarji‘) that amount from the father once he returns. This is an obligation on the mother due to the child's need, with the right of recourse. Second, the commentator deduces from this the general procedure for the judge: when the husband is absent and leaves nothing for his wife and children, the judge first hears evidence from the wife regarding the marriage (if he is not sure), then he fixes maintenance (farḍ) for her and her children, then orders her to borrow (ista'dāna). When the husband returns, she reclaims the fixed amount from him. The phrase 'upon it is the practice' (‘alayhi al-‘amal) indicates this is the operative procedure for judges. The authority weight here is from the later fatwa works (al-Khaniya is later).

Key Terms
تجبر (tujbar): She is compelled by the judge to spend on her children.
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Borrowing money for maintenance, here ordered by the judge.

D) He contacts the husband to send money

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Depositary and Debtor Spending Without Judge's Order
Comments on the word 'farḍ' and two cases of spending without order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal consequences when a depositary versus a debtor spends on maintenance without a judge's order and explain the basis for each ruling.

Core Idea
Without a judge's order, the depositary is liable and the debtor remains liable — spending without authority does not transfer liability.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq comments on the word 'farḍ' (fixing) in Kanz al-Daqā'iq, addressing a critical distinction in maintenance law. When a depositary (holding a deposit from an absent husband) or a debtor (owing a debt to the husband) spends on the wife's maintenance without judicial authorization, their legal positions differ sharply. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about liability and recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Recognize the depositary (mūdi‘) as the person holding a deposit from the absent husband, and the debtor (madīūn) as the person who owes a debt to the absent husband.
Both are third parties spending on the wife's maintenance without a judge's order.
2
State the ruling for the depositary
The passage states that if the depositary spends without order, he is liable (ḍāmin). This means he cannot recover from the husband and his own deposit is lost.
The depositary's unauthorized spending makes him a guarantor.
3
State the ruling for the debtor
The debtor is not absolved (lā yabra’) – his debt remains unchanged. He cannot deduct what he spent from his obligation.
Spending on maintenance does not count as repayment unless ordered or authorized.
4
Note the common consequence: no recourse
Both the depositary and debtor have no right of recourse (lā rujū‘) against the person on whom they spent (the wife).
The wife keeps the maintenance and is not liable to repay.
5
Understand the analogy and the judge's role
The commentator compares the depositary to one who uses the deposit to pay the depositor's debt without order – both are liable. He then notes that in this issue, the judge's order does not change the rule because the judge cannot authorize payment of the absent person's debt from his deposit.
This underscores that the rule is based on lack of authority, not the presence or absence of a judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بقوله «فرض» إلى أن المودع والمديون لو أنفقا بغير أمر القاضي فإن المودع ضامن ولا يبرأ المديون، ولا رجوع للمنفق على من أنفق عليه كما في الذخيرة وجعله في الخانية نظير المودع لو قضى بالوديعة دين المودع بغير أمر القاضي فإنه يكون ضامناً .اهـ. مع أنه في هذه المسألة لا فرق بين أمر القاضي وعدمه فإنه ليس للقاضي أن يقضي دين الغائب من وديعته كما قدمناه، ولم يذكر المصنف الحكم بعد حضور الزوج.
And he indicated by his saying 'farḍ' that if the depositary and the debtor spend without the judge's order, then the depositary is liable and the debtor is not absolved, and the spender has no recourse against the one on whom he spent, as in al-Dhakhira. And he made it in al-Khaniya analogous to the depositary who discharges the depositor's debt with the deposit without the judge's order, for he is liable. However, in this issue there is no difference between the judge's order and its absence, for the judge is not authorized to discharge the absent person's debt from his deposit as we have mentioned. And the author did not mention the ruling after the husband's return.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the word 'farḍ' (fixing) in the matn. It explains a crucial distinction between two parties who might spend on the wife's maintenance without a judge's order: the depositary (mūdi‘) and the debtor (madīūn). The ruling is that if the depositary spends, he is liable (ḍāmin) – meaning he cannot recover from the husband and his own deposit is lost. The debtor, on the other hand, is not absolved (lā yabra’ al-madīūn) – meaning his debt remains, and he cannot deduct what he spent. Furthermore, the spender (i.e., the depositary or debtor) has no right of recourse (‘adam al-rujū‘) against the person he spent on (the wife). The commentator compares the depositary's situation to one who uses the deposit to pay the depositor's debt without order – in both cases, he is liable. Then he notes that in this issue, the ruling is the same whether there is a judge's order or not, because the judge does not have the authority to pay the absent person's debt from his deposit. Finally, the commentator notes that the author (of Kanz al-Daqā'iq) did not mention the ruling after the husband returns (ḥukm ba‘da ḥuḍūr al-zawj). This sets up the following discussion.

Key Terms
المودع (al-mūdi‘): The person who holds a deposit (wadī‘a) belonging to the absent husband.
المديون (al-madīūn): The person who owes a debt to the absent husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for a depositary who spends on the wife's maintenance without a judge's order?
A) He becomes a creditor of the husband
B) He is liable (ḍāmin) and cannot reclaim
C) The wife must repay him
D) The husband's deposit is reduced

Key Takeaway
Without a judge's order, a depositary becomes liable for unauthorized spending, while a debtor's obligation remains unchanged — both lose any right of recourse.

The Husband's Return and the Guarantor's Liability: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute about guarantor liability after wife's oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on whether the husband can recover maintenance from the guarantor after the wife's oath, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The guarantor's liability depends on when the guarantee was given; the wife's oath clears the guarantor if the guarantee was before the second borrowing.

This passage discusses a nuanced scenario: an absent husband returns and claims he already paid maintenance. The wife may have borrowed with a guarantor. The central dispute is whether the husband can pursue the guarantor after the wife swears she did not receive the money. Al-Dhakhira and al-Mabsut offer different rulings, and the commentator resolves the ikhtilaf by analyzing the type of kafala contract.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the legal situation: an absent husband returns and claims he already paid or sent maintenance. The wife is the recipient, and there is a guarantor for her borrowing.
The scenario assumes the wife had to borrow due to non-receipt of maintenance.
2
State the Ruling in al-Dhakhira
According to al-Dhakhira, if the husband provides evidence of payment, the wife must return what she took. If he has no evidence and the wife swears, the guarantor is free. If she refuses to swear, both she and the guarantor are liable, and the husband can choose whom to take from.
This is one position within the Hanafi school.
3
State the Opposing View in al-Mabsut
The view in al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi is that even if the wife swears, the husband can only take from the wife, not from the guarantor.
This is the preferred position according to the commentator.
4
Analyse the Dalil: Nature of the Guarantee
The passage explains that the guarantee (kafala) only covers a debt existing at the time of the guarantee. When the wife took the second loan (after the guarantee), that debt existed at the time of guarantee, so it is of the first type (kafala for an existing debt). The guarantor is not liable for the first borrowing because the guarantee was only for the second.
The key distinction: kafala for a debt existing at present vs. kafala for a future debt.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The commentator sides with al-Mabsut and al-Mujtaba, stating that the husband can only take from the wife, not the guarantor. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: in one view the husband has a choice; in the other he is restricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الذخيرة فإن حضر الزوج وقال كنت أوفيت النفقة أو أرسلت إليها النفقة فالقاضي يقول له أقم البينة فإن أقامها أمرها القاضي برد ما أخذت لأنه ظهر عند القاضي أنها أخذت بغير حق وللزوج الخيار إن شاء أخذها بذلك وإن شاء آخذ الكفيل فإن لم يكن للزوج بينة وحلفت المرأة على ذلك فلا شيء على الكفيل، وإن نكلت عن اليمين ونكل الكفيل لزمهما المال وللزوج الخيار فقد ذكر في هذه المسألة نكولهما ونكول المرأة أمر لازم، وأما نكول الكفيل فليس بلازم بل إذا نكلت المرأة فذلك يكفي لثبوت الخيار للزوج وإن لم ينكل الكفيل لأن النكول إقرار والأصيل إذا أقر بالمال لزم الكفيل وإن جحد الكفيل ولا ضمان على المودع لأن أمر القاضي بالدفع إليها قد صح فصار كأمره بنفسه .اهـ. ويخالفه ما في المبسوط وشرح الطحاوي من أنها لو أقرت أنها الفرق بين الأصيل وبين من تعجلت نفقتها فالزوج يأخذه من المرأة ولا يأخذه من الكفيل .اهـ. وسيأتي في باب الكفالة الكفالة بدين قائم في الحال كقوله كفلت بمالك عليه فلا يلزم الكفيل ما أقر به الكفالة بدين يجب كقوله ما ثبت لك عليه أو ذاب فيلزم الكفيل كما في فتح القدير. ولا يخفى أن الكفيل إنما ضمن الدين القائم للحال لأنها لما أخذت ثانياً ضمنها فكان وقت الضمان الدين قائم في ذمتها للحال وهو ما أخذته ثانياً، فظهر بهذا أنه القسم الأول، فالحق ما في المبسوط كما في المجتبى ولم يذكر أنه يأخذ منها كفيلاً بنفسها أو بما أعطاها. وذكر في شس: فإذا حلفت فأعطاها النفقة أخذ منها كفيلاً بذلك . بط : وهو الصحيح اهـ. فقد صرح بأن الكفالة إنما هو بما أخذته قبل الكفالة فهو نظير قوله كفلت بمالك عليه. وفي الخانية وبعد ما أمر القاضي المودع أو المديون إذا قال المودع دفعت المال إليها لأجل النفقة قبل قوله ولا يقبل قول المديون إلا ببينة اهـ. ولم يذكر قولها وينبغي أن يكون كالبينة لأنها مقرة على نفسها. وفي الخانية والوديعة أولى من الدين في البداءة بالإنفاق منها عليها. وفي الذخيرة وينفق القاضي عليها من غلة الدار والعبد الذي للغائب لأنه هو من جنس حقها. وأطلق المصنف في الغائب فشمل المفقود وغيره كما في شرح الطحاوي ولم يقيد فيما عندي من الكتب الغيبة بشيء إلا في الفتاوى الصيرفية فإنه قال : إيجاب النفقة في مال الغائب يشترط أن يكون مدة سفر اهـ. وهو قيد حسن يجب حفظه فإنه فيما دونه ويسهل إحصاره ومراجعته .
He said in al-Dhakhira: If the husband returns and says 'I had given the maintenance or sent it to her', the judge tells him to produce evidence. If he produces it, the judge orders her to return what she took because it becomes apparent that she took it without right. The husband has the option: if he wishes, he takes it from her, or if he wishes, he takes the guarantor. If the husband has no evidence and the wife swears, then nothing is due from the guarantor. If she refuses to swear and the guarantor also refuses, then both are liable for the money and the husband has the option. It has been mentioned in this issue that their refusal (nukūl) and the wife's refusal is a binding matter; as for the guarantor's refusal, it is not binding. Rather, if the wife refuses, that suffices to establish the husband's option even if the guarantor does not refuse, because refusal is an admission, and when the principal admits to the money, the guarantor becomes liable even if he denies it. And there is no liability on the depositary because the judge's order to pay her is valid, so it is as if he himself ordered. This is contradicted by what is in al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi: that if she admits that... the difference between the principal and one who... so the husband takes it from the woman and not from the guarantor. It will come in the chapter of kafala: kafala for a debt existing at present, like saying 'I guarantee your right against him' does not oblige the guarantor for what he admits; kafala for a debt that must be, like saying 'what is established for you or remains', obliges the guarantor as in Fath al-Qadir. It is not hidden that the guarantor only guarantees the existing debt at present because when she took the second time, he guaranteed her, so at the time of guarantee the debt existed in her liability at present, which is what she took the second time. Thus it appears that it is the first type, so the truth is in al-Mabsut as in al-Mujtaba, and he did not mention that he takes a guarantor from her herself or from what he gave her. And he mentioned in Shas: If she swore, then he gave her the maintenance, he took a guarantor from her for that. Bt: it is the correct view. So he explicitly stated that the guarantee is only for what she took before the guarantee, so it is analogous to his saying 'I guarantee your right against him'. In al-Khaniya: After the judge orders the depositary or debtor, if the depositary says 'I paid the money to her for maintenance', his word is accepted, but the word of the debtor is not accepted except with evidence. And he did not mention her statement, and it should be like evidence because she is confessing against herself. In al-Khaniya: The deposit is preferred over the debt in beginning to spend from it. In al-Dhakhira: The judge spends on her from the income of the house and the slave of the absent person because it is of the same type as her right. And the author (al-Mawsili) used the term 'absent' generally, including the missing person (mafqūd) and others, as in Sharh al-Tahawi, and he did not restrict absence in any of the books I have except in Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya, where it says: 'Imposing maintenance from the property of the absent person requires that his absence be for the duration of a journey (safar)'. This is a good restriction that must be remembered, for in what is less than that, it is easy to locate and contact him.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This long passage from the commentary deals with the scenario when the husband returns after his wife borrowed for maintenance. It begins by quoting al-Dhakhīra: If the husband returns and claims he already paid or sent maintenance, the judge asks him for evidence. If he provides it, the wife must return what she took. The husband has the option to take the money from the wife or from the guarantor (kafīl). If the husband has no evidence and the wife swears she did not receive it, the guarantor is free of liability. If the wife refuses to swear and the guarantor also refuses, both are liable. The commentator then notes a difference of opinion: al-Mabsūt and Sharh al-Tahāwī hold that the husband can only take from the wife, not the guarantor. The passage resolves this by analyzing the nature of the guarantee: the guarantor only guarantees the debt that existed at the time of the guarantee, which is the amount the wife took after the guarantee. This aligns with the view in al-Mabsūt. The passage also discusses related issues: the acceptance of the depositary's word versus the debtor's, the preference of the deposit over debt, and the general rule that the judge can spend from the absent husband's property. A key point is that the condition for imposing maintenance from the absent's property is that the absence must be for a travel distance (safar), as stated in Fatāwā al-Ṣayrafiyya. This is a useful condition to remember. The authority weight is from later fatwa works, and the tarjīh favors the view in al-Mabsūt.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafīl): The guarantor who guarantees the wife's repayment of borrowed maintenance to the husband.
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath when offered.
كفالة بدين قائم (kafāla bi-dayn qā'im): Guarantee for a debt that exists at the time of the guarantee contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the wife only
B) From the guarantor only
C) From both the wife and the guarantor
D) From neither, because the oath resolves the matter

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the guarantor's liability is limited to the debt existing at the time of guarantee; the wife's oath discharges him unless she refuses to swear.

Mother's Non-Recourse for Court-Authorized Spending on Minor Son
Subsidiary ruling with textual refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mother cannot reclaim from her son money she borrowed with the judge's order for his maintenance during minority, and identify the underlying conditions.

Core Idea
A mother's court-authorized borrowing for a minor son is non-recoverable; the son owes nothing after maturity.

This subsidiary ruling addresses a practical scenario: a mother borrows money to feed her young son because both are destitute, with a judge's order. After the son grows up, can she demand repayment? The Hanafi answer is clear — no. The passage also clarifies a nuanced point about oaths and guarantors, teaching us how precise legal language avoids false inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Ruling
Read the scenario: a mother has a young son with no money, the mother also has no money, she borrows with a judge's order and spends on the son. The ruling: la tarja‘u ‘alayhi bi-dhalik (she does not reclaim from him for that).
The ruling is explicit and unconditional.
2
Identify the Conditions
Distill the conditions that trigger this ruling: (1) the son is a minor, (2) both mother and son have no money, (3) the mother borrows with a judge's order, (4) she spends on the son's maintenance.
If any condition is missing, the ruling may differ.
3
Understand the Legal Consequence
After the son reaches maturity (balagha), the mother cannot reclaim the borrowed amount. The judge's order was for immediate necessity, and the son is not liable for expenses incurred during his minority when the mother was obliged.
This is based on the principle that the mother's duty to maintain her child (n afaqah) is absolute when the child has no wealth.
4
Examine the Guarantor/Oath Discussion
The commentator refutes a potential misunderstanding: the phrase 'there is nothing on the guarantor' does not imply the husband has automatic recourse against the wife without an oath. It simply removes the false inference that the guarantor might be liable.
This shows the precision required in legal language.
5
Analyze the Correction of al-‘Alā'ī
The commentator corrects al-‘Alā'ī's wording in al-Durr al-Mukhtār: 'if she swears, she alone is sued' should be 'if she admits, she alone is sued'. This agrees with al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi.
Reflect on the difference between admission and oath in legal proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع امرأة لها ابن صغير لا مال له ولا للمرأة فاستدانت وأنفقت على الصغير بأمر القاضي فبلغ لا ترجع عليه بذلك. تتارخانية قوله : فلا شيء على الكفيل) مفهومه أن للزوج الرجوع عليها ولا وجه له وإلا كان له الرجوع عليها بدون تحليف ولو كان كذلك لم يحتج للأمر بإقامة البينة للرجوع عليها، والظاهر أنه نص على أنه لا شيء على الكفيل لأنه لم يحلف فربما يتوهم أنه يرجع عليه فنص على عدمه لدفع ذلك التوهم. أو المراد أنه لا تحليف على الكفيل بل يبرأ بحلفها بدون تحليفه. وبهذا اندفع ما فهمه العلائي في الدر المختار حيث قال : ولو حلفت طولبت فقط ولم يعزه لأحد ولعله سبق قلم، ومراده أن يقول: ولو أقرت طولبت فقط فإنه موافق لما يأتي عن المبسوط وشرح الطحاوي فليتأمل .
Subsidiary issue: A woman has a young son with no money, and the woman also has no money, so she borrows and spends on the son by the judge's order, then the son grows up; she does not reclaim from him for that. [From] Tatarkhaniyya: His saying: 'then there is nothing on the guarantor' implies that the husband has recourse against her, but there is no basis for it; otherwise he would have recourse against her without an oath, and if that were the case, he would not need to order her to produce evidence for recourse. Apparently he stated that there is nothing on the guarantor because he did not swear, so perhaps one might think he has recourse against him, so he stated its absence to remove that misunderstanding. Or it means that there is no oath on the guarantor; rather, he is absolved by her oath without his oath. And by this is refuted what al-‘Alā'ī understood in al-Durr al-Mukhtār where he said: 'If she swears, she is alone sued' and he did not attribute it to anyone, perhaps it is a slip of the pen. What he meant is: 'If she admits, she alone is sued' because that agrees with what will come from al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi. Reflect.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This is a subsidiary issue (far‘) from the text. It presents a scenario: a woman has a young son (ibn saghīr) who has no money, and the woman also has no money. She borrows money with the judge's order and spends it on the son. Later, the son grows up (balagha) and becomes capable of earning. The ruling is that she cannot reclaim (lā tarja‘u) from the son the amount she spent. This is because the judge's order was for immediate necessity, and the son is not liable for maintenance incurred during his minority when the mother was obliged to spend. The passage then comments on the phrase 'there is nothing on the guarantor' from the previous discussion. It clarifies that this does not mean the husband has recourse against the wife without an oath; rather, it removes the misunderstanding that the guarantor might be liable. The commentator also corrects a misinterpretation by al-‘Alā'ī in al-Durr al-Mukhtār, stating that the correct expression is 'if she admits, she alone is sued' not 'if she swears'. This is a technical refinement of the legal language.

Key Terms
فرع (far‘): A subsidiary legal issue derived from the main topic.
بلغ (balagha): The son reaches physical maturity (bulūgh), making him responsible for his own maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the subsidiary issue, after the son grows up, can the mother reclaim the money she borrowed to spend on him with the judge's order?
A) Yes, because the son is now responsible
B) No, she cannot reclaim
C) Only if the judge orders it
D) Only if the son agrees

Key Takeaway
A mother's court-authorized borrowing for a minor son's maintenance creates no debt for the son; she cannot reclaim it after his maturity.

Preference of Deposit over Debt and Condition of Travel in Nafaqah
Annotation of two legal points in nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why deposit is preferred over debt for maintenance and identify the condition of travel required for imposing maintenance from an absent husband's property.

Core Idea
Deposit is preferred over debt for maintenance because it risks destruction; absence must be a travel distance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq clarifies two practical points in the law of maintenance (nafaqah) from an absent husband's property. The commentator explains why a deposit (wadī‘a) takes priority over a debt (dayn) when the judge orders spending, and then refines the condition of the husband's absence by quoting Fatawa al-Ṣayrafiyya: the absence must be for the duration of a journey.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings being annotated
The passage comments on two statements from the matn: first, preference of deposit over debt; second, an exception from Fatawa al-Ṣayrafiyya regarding the condition of absence.
2
Understand the reasoning for deposit preference
The deposit is susceptible to destruction (tahtamilu al-halāk), so spending from it first preserves the debt, which remains a liability regardless.
Compare with debt: debt is a certainty, deposit is a trust exposed to risk.
3
Extract the condition from Fatawa al-Ṣayrafiyya
The condition is that the absence must be for the duration of a journey (muddat safar). This is the operative condition for the judge to impose maintenance from the absent's property.
Some Hanafi works omit this condition, but later fatwas specify it.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi discussion
Al-Ramli observes that the condition is explicit in al-Tatarkhāniyya but some left it out because it seemed obvious from the reasoning. The commentator adds a reference to al-Qahistānī.
This shows how later scholars refine and comment on earlier rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والوديعة أولى من الدين في البدأة لأنها تحتمل الهلاك بخلاف الدين؛ كذا في التتارخانية . قوله : (إلا في فتاوى الصيرفية الخ) قال الرملي : وقد صرح بها في التتارخانية نقلاً عن فتاوى آهو والظاهر أنهم إنما تركوه لظهوره من التعليل تأمل ..اهـ :قلت لكن في القهستاني : ويفرض
His saying: (And the deposit is preferred over the debt in starting because it is susceptible to destruction contrary to the debt; thus in al-Tatarkhaniyya. His saying: (Except in Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya etc.) al-Ramli said: And it is explicit in al-Tatarkhaniyya quoted from Fatawa Ahu, and apparently they left it due to its obviousness from the reasoning, reflect... I said: But in al-Qahistani: and he fixes
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
These are two annotations (qawluh) on the text being commented. The first annotation explains why the deposit (wadī‘a) is preferred over the debt (dayn) when starting to spend on the wife's maintenance: because the deposit is susceptible to destruction (tahtamilu al-halāk) if not used promptly, whereas the debt remains owed regardless. Thus, the judge should first order spending from the deposit if available. The second annotation comments on the condition for imposing maintenance from the absent husband's property, as mentioned earlier: according to Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya, the absence must be for the duration of a journey (safar). Al-Ramli notes that this condition is explicit in al-Tatarkhaniyya but some omitted it because it is obvious from the reasoning. The commentator then adds a note from al-Qahistani that seems to continue the idea. These annotations serve to refine the understanding of the primary text.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī‘a): A deposit of property held by a depositary for the owner.
فتاوى الصيرفية (Fatawa al-Ṣayrafiyya): A collection of fatwas by Imam al-Ṣayrafi, specifying conditions for maintenance from absent property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is deposit preferred over debt for maintenance?
A) Because deposit is more valuable
B) Because deposit is susceptible to destruction
C) Because debt is owed to the wife
D) Because deposit is a form of charity

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi nafaqah, deposit takes priority over debt due to risk of loss, and maintenance from an absent husband's property requires the absence to be a travel distance.

Nafaqah for a Poor Child: Obligation and Definition of 'Tifl'
Ruling with proof and terminological expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a poor child, identify the Quranic proof, and explain the refined definition of 'tifl' by Al-Ramli.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is wajib for a child who cannot earn; the father alone bears it.

This lesson examines the obligation of a father to provide maintenance for his young, poor child. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states the ruling, and the commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the meaning of 'tifl' and provides Quranic evidence. We will explore the proof and the important refinement by Al-Ramli regarding the child's ability to earn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State that nafaqah, housing, and clothing are obligatory for a poor child, and note the term 'tifl' used in the matn.
The matn says 'waladihi al-faqir' – his poor child.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof
The commentator cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:233. Explain how the verse about wives indicates the father's obligation for children (isharah).
The dalil is qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah.
3
Examine the expansion on 'tifl'
The commentator describes additional implications: father's exclusive liability, no qisas/hadd due to child. Then Al-Ramli refines that 'tifl' means the child unable to earn.
If the child can earn, the father's nafaqah is not wajib.
4
Understand the consequences
The father bears nafaqah alone; if child is wealthy and father needy, no one shares father's obligation.
This is from Sharh al-Manar.
5
Apply the ruling
Use the conditions (child poor, unable to earn) to determine obligation in a scenario.
Focus on earning capacity as the key condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولطفله الفقير) أي تجب النفقة والسكنى والكسوة لولده الصغير الفقير لقوله تعالى ﴿وعلى المولود له رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف﴾ [البقرة: ۲۳۳] فهي عبارة في إيجاب نفقة المنكوحات إشارة إلى أن نفقة الأولاد على الأب وأن النسب له وأنه لا يعاقب بسببه فلا يقتل قصاصاً بقتله ولا يحد بوطء جاريته وإن علم بحرمتها، وأن الأب ينفرد بتحمل نفقة الولد ولا يشاركه فيها أحد، وأن الولد إذا كان غنياً والأب محتاجاً لم يشارك الولد أحد في نفقة الوالد، ذكره المصنف في شرح المنار . قيد بالطفل وهو الصبي حين يسقط من البطن قوله : (وقيد بالطفل إلى قوله لأن البالغ قال الرملي في هذه العبارة نظر وحق العبارة أن يقال : أراد بالطفل العاجز عن التكسب لأنه إذا بلغ حد الكسب ولم يبلغ في نفسه لا تجب على أبيه نفقته بل يؤجر وينفق عليه من أجرته وسيصرح به قريباً. هذا وقد قال العلقمي في شرح الجامع الصغير قال بعضهم: يبقى هذا الاسم للولد حتى يميز ثم لا يقال له بعد طفل بل صبي
His statement: '(and for his poor child)' means nafaqah, housing, and clothing are obligatory for his poor young child, due to the saying of Allah Most High: 'And upon the father is their provision and clothing according to what is acceptable.' [2:233] This is an expression concerning the obligation of nafaqah for wives, indicating that the nafaqah of children is upon the father, and that lineage belongs to him, and that he is not punished because of the child—so he is not killed in qisas for killing him, nor is he subject to hadd for intercourse with his slave girl even if he knows its prohibition—and that the father alone bears the nafaqah of the child, no one shares with him, and that if the child is wealthy and the father is needy, no one shares with the father in the nafaqah of the child. The author mentioned this in Sharh al-Manar. He restricted it to 'child' (tifl), which is the boy when he falls from the womb. His statement: (And he restricted it to 'child' until his saying: because the adult) – al-Ramli said: In this expression there is consideration; the correct expression is to say: He meant by 'child' the one incapable of earning, because if he reaches the age of earning but not [maturity] himself, his nafaqah is not obligatory on his father; rather, he should be hired out and spent from his wages, and he will soon state this. And al-'Alqami said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: Some said: This name (tifl) remains for the child until he reaches discernment (tamyiz); after that he is not called a child but a boy (sabi).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran on the obligation of nafaqah for a poor child. The commentator explains that the phrase 'waladihi al-faqir' in the matn refers to the father's obligation to provide nafaqah, housing (sukna), and clothing (kiswa) for his young, poor child. He cites Quran 2:233, which in its apparent meaning speaks of wives, but the commentator notes that it indicates that children's nafaqah is on the father, as the father is responsible for the child's lineage. He also mentions that the father does not face qisas or hadd due to the child, and that the father exclusively bears child support. If the child is wealthy and the father poor, no one else shares the father's obligation to support the child. The commentator then clarifies the term 'tifl' (child): it is the young child from birth. Al-Ramli notes that the intended meaning is the child who cannot earn; if the child can earn (e.g., by working) but is not yet mature, the father is not obligated to provide nafaqah; instead, the child's earnings should be used. Al-'Alqami adds that the term 'tifl' is used until the child reaches the age of discernment (tamyiz), after which he is called a boy (sabi). The passage provides the foundational ruling and clarifies the scope of the term.

Key Terms
طفل (tifl): A young child from birth until the age of discernment (tamyiz), unable to earn.
نفقة الولد (nafaqat al-walad): Maintenance of the child, including food, shelter, and clothing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a father not obligated to provide nafaqah for his child?
A) When the child is wealthy.
B) When the child is capable of earning but not yet mature.
C) When the mother is wealthy.
D) When the child lives with maternal grandparents.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah for a poor child is wajib on the father, but only for a child who cannot earn; the father's obligation is exclusive.

Maintenance of a Poor Minor Child: Obligation and Details
Commentary on father's nafaqah for child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father is obligated to maintain his poor minor child and apply the ruling to various financial scenarios.

Core Idea
A father must maintain his poor minor child; the obligation continues until the child can earn.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the father's duty to provide for his poor minor child. The ruling is derived from the Quran and elaborated with specific definitions and case-based rules. We will work through the proof, the definitions of 'child' and 'poor', and then explore the different financial situations that affect who pays.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proof
Locate the ruling statement (nafaqah, suknah, kiswah) and the Quranic verse (2:233). Understand how the verse is used as an isharah that children's maintenance is on the father.
The verse primarily addresses mothers but indicates the father's responsibility.
2
Define 'tifl' and 'faqir' in the Hanafi context
Analyze why 'tifl' is defined as one incapable of earning, and 'faqir' as one without sufficient wealth. Note the disagreement on the age limit and the preferred definition.
The term 'tifl' includes both male and female until the age of earning.
3
Analyze the financial scenarios
Work through the four combinations of father/child wealth: both rich, both poor, father rich/child poor, father poor/child rich. Determine who pays in each case.
When child has wealth, maintenance is from that wealth; father may use it with permission.
4
Examine special rulings: hiring out and the wasteful father
Understand the father's right to hire out a male child for maintenance after had al-kasb, and restrictions when the father is mubadhdhir (wasteful).
This right does not apply to female children.
5
Consider edge cases: child's absent wealth, waqf property, and mother's role
Reflect on the questions raised about a child's entitlement in waqf and the mother's authority to sell the child's house for maintenance with judicial permission.
The passage leaves some issues for further pondering (tammul).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولطفله الفقير) أي تجب النفقة والسكنى والكسوة لولده الصغير الفقير لقوله تعالى وعلى المولود له رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۳۳] فهي عبارة في إيجاب نفقة المنكوحات إشارة إلى أن نفقة الأولاد على الأب وأن النسب له وأنه لا يعاقب بسببه فلا يقتل قصاصاً بقتله ولا يحد بوطء جاريته وإن علم بحرمتها، وأن الأب ينفرد بتحمل نفقة الولد ولا يشاركه فيها أحد، وأن الولد إذا كان غنياً والأب محتاجاً لم يشارك الولد أحد في نفقة الوالد، ذكره المصنف في شرح المنار . قيد بالطفل وهو الصبي حين يسقط من البطن قوله : (وقيد بالطفل إلى قوله لأن البالغ قال الرملي في هذه العبارة نظر وحق العبارة أن يقال : أراد بالطفل العاجز عن التكسب لأنه إذا بلغ حد الكسب ولم يبلغ في نفسه لا تجب على أبيه نفقته بل يؤجر وينفق عليه من أجرته وسيصرح به قريباً. هذا وقد قال العلقمي في شرح الجامع الصغير قال بعضهم: يبقى هذا الاسم للولد حتى يميز ثم لا يقال له بعد طفل بل صبي وخرور إلى أن يحتلم ويقال جارية طفل وطفلة كذا في المغرب وبه علم أن الطفل يقع على الذكر والأنثى ولذا عبر به لأن البالغ لا تجب نفقته على أبيه إلا بشروط نذكرها. وقيد بالفقير لأن الصغير إذا كان له مال فنفقته في ماله ولا بد من التقييد بالحرية لما أسلفناه أن الولد المملوك نفقته على مالكه لا على أبيه حراً كان الأب أو عبداً. والحاصل أن الأب لا يخلو إما أن يكون غنياً أو فقيراً والصغير كذلك، فإن كان الأب والصغير غنيين فإن الأب ينفق عليه من مال نفسه إن كان حاضراً، وإن كان مال الصغير غائباً وجبت على الأب، فإذا أراد الرجوع أنفق عليه بإذن القاضي، فلو أنفق بلا أمره ليس له الرجوع في الحكم إلا أن يكون أشهد أنه أنفق ليرجع، ولو لم يشهد لكنه أنفق بنية الرجوع لم يكن له رجوع في الحكم وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى يحل له الرجوع. وإن كان للصغير عقاراً وأردية أو ثياب واحتيج إلى النفقة كان للأب أن [؟] ذلك كله وينفق عليه لأنه غني بهذه الأشياء. وإن كانا فقيرين فعند الخصاف أن يبيع الأب بتكفف الناس وينفق على أولاده الصغار، وقيل نفقتهم في بيت المال. هذا إذا كان عاجزاً عن الكسب وإن كان قادراً على الكسب اكتسب وأنفق فإن امتنع عن الكسب حبس بخلاف سائر الديون. ولا يحبس والد إن علا في دين ولده وإن سفل إلا في النفقة لأن في الامتناع عن الإنفاق إتلاف النفس، وإذا لم يف كسبه بحاجته أو لم يكتسب لعدم تيسره أنفق عليهم القريب ورجع على الأب إذا أيسر وإن كان الأب غنياً والولد الصغير فقيراً فالنفقة على الأب إلى أن يبلغ الذكر حد الكسب وإن لم يبلغ الحلم، فإذا كان هذا كان للأب أن يؤاجره وينفق عليه من أجرته وليس له في الأنثى ذلك، فلو كان الأب مبذراً يدفع كسب الابن إلى أمين كما في سائر أملاكه، وإن كان الأب فقيراً والصغير غنياً لا تجب نفقته على أبيه بل نفقة أبيه عليه ؛ كذا في الذخيرة وذكر الولوالجي أن في كل موضع أوجبنا نفقة الولد فإنه يدخل ويافع ومرهق وبالغ. وما قاله بعضهم هو المعروف الآن في بلادنا والمشهور فيما بينهم فعليه تحصل غاية المناسبة في الشرح أن يقال أراد بالطفل العاجز عن الكسب الخ فتأمل قوله : (وإن كان مال الصغير غائباً الخ) أقول: وقد سئلت عن صبي لا مال له غير أن له استحقاقاً في غلة وقف هل يكون بمنزلة ماله الغائب أو يكون بمنزلة من لا مال له أصلا؟ ولم أر من صرح بالمسألة والظاهر الأول حتى إذا أنفق بإذن القاضي له الرجوع فليتأمل رملي قوله : ( وإن كان للصغير عقار الخ) أقول : ومثل الأب في ذلك الأم وهي واقعة الفتوى إذا أمر القاضي أمهم بالإنفاق عليهم وليس لهم سوى حصة من دار يسكنونها هل تباع في نفقتهم أم لا؟ والذي يظهر أنها تباع في ذلك وتنفق عليهم من ثمنها والسكنى من النفقة وإذا فرغ وجبت عليها رملي أقول : الظاهر أن مراد المؤلف بقوله «وإن كان له عقار الخ» إذا كان الصغير لا يحتاج إلى ذلك، أما إذا كان محتاجاً لسكنى عقاره ولبس ثيابه وأرديته لا فائدة في بيع ذلك لأنه لو باعها يحتاج إلى شراء غيرها وانظر ما سيذكره المؤلف عن البدائع في شرح قوله ولفقير محرم من أن الفقير من تحل له الصدقة وأنه لو كان له عقار وخادم
His statement: (And for his poor child) meaning: maintenance, lodging, and clothing are obligatory for his young poor child, due to Allah's statement: 'And upon the father is their provision and clothing according to what is acceptable' [Al-Baqarah: 233]. This expression is about obligating maintenance for married women, indicating that the maintenance of children is upon the father, and that lineage belongs to him, and that he is not punished because of him—so he is not killed in retaliation for killing him, nor is he punished for having intercourse with his slave girl even if he knows her unlawfulness. And that the father alone bears the maintenance of the child, no one shares with him. And that if the child is wealthy and the father needy, no one shares with the child in the maintenance of the parent. The author mentioned this in Sharh al-Manar. It is restricted to 'child' (tifl), which is the male when he falls from the belly. His statement: (And restricted to child ... because the adult). Al-Ramli said: In this expression there is a consideration; the proper expression is to say: He meant by 'child' the one incapable of earning, because when he reaches the age of earning and has not yet reached puberty, his maintenance is not obligatory upon his father; rather he is hired out and maintenance is provided from his wage, as he will state shortly. Al-‘Alqami said in Sharh al-Jami‘ al-Saghir that some said: This name remains for the child until he distinguishes, then after that he is not called a child but a boy (sabi) and [kharur?] until he reaches puberty, and it is said a girl (jariyah) child (tifl) and (tiflah). Likewise in al-Mughrib. And from this it is known that 'child' applies to male and female, so the author used it. Because the adult's maintenance is not obligatory upon his father except under conditions we will mention. It is restricted to 'poor' because if the young child has wealth, his maintenance is from his wealth. And it must be restricted to freedom, as we previously stated that the owned child's maintenance is upon his owner, not upon his father, whether the father is free or a slave. In summary: the father is either rich or poor, and the child likewise. If both the father and child are rich, the father spends on him from his own wealth if it is present. If the child's wealth is absent, it becomes obligatory upon the father; if he wants to seek reimbursement, he should spend with the permission of the judge. If he spends without his order, he cannot seek reimbursement in judgment unless he has witnesses that he spent with the intention of reimbursement. If he did not have witnesses but spent with the intention of reimbursement, he cannot seek reimbursement in judgment, but between him and Allah, it is permissible for him to seek reimbursement. If the child has real estate and garments or clothes and needs maintenance, the father may [?] all of that and spend on him because he is wealthy with these things. If both are poor, according to al-Khassaf: the father should beg from people and spend on his young children. It is said: their maintenance is from the public treasury. This is if he is incapable of earning; if he is able to earn, he earns and spends. If he refrains from earning, he is imprisoned, unlike other debts. A father, even if high, is not imprisoned for his child's debt, even if low, except for maintenance, because refraining from spending is causing destruction of life. If his earnings do not suffice his need, or he does not earn due to lack of means, the relative spends on them and seeks reimbursement from the father when he becomes wealthy. If the father is rich and the young child is poor, maintenance is upon the father until the male reaches the age of earning even if he has not reached puberty. When this happens, the father may hire him out and spend on him from his wage, but he does not have that right regarding the female. If the father is wasteful (mubadhdhir), he gives the son’s earnings to a trustee, as in his other properties. If the father is poor and the child is rich, the child’s maintenance is not obligatory upon the father; rather the father’s maintenance is upon the child. This is in al-Dhakhirah. Al-Walwaliji mentioned that wherever we have obligated the child’s maintenance, it includes [the child] when he is [yafi‘?] and [murhiq?] and adult. And what some have said is what is known now in our lands and famous among them. So based on this, the full appropriateness in the commentary is to say: He meant by 'child' the one incapable of earning, etc. So ponder. His statement: (And if the young child's wealth is absent, etc.) I say: I have been asked about a boy who has no wealth except an entitlement in the yield of a waqf: does it become like his absent wealth, or like one who has no wealth at all? I have not seen anyone who clarified the issue. The apparent is the first, so that if he spends with the judge's permission, he can seek reimbursement. So let it be pondered. Al-Ramli. His statement: (And if the young child has real estate, etc.) I say: The mother is like the father in that. And it is the reality of the fatwa: if the judge orders the mother to spend on them, and they have only a share of a house they reside in, is it sold for their maintenance or not? What appears is that it is sold for that and they are spent on from its price, and residence is part of maintenance. And when it is finished, it becomes obligatory upon her. Al-Ramli. I say: The apparent is that the author’s meaning by his statement 'And if he has real estate, etc.' is when the young child does not need that. But if he needs to reside in his real estate and wear his clothes and garments, selling them is of no benefit because if he sells them, he will need to buy others. And see what the author will mention from al-Bada'i‘ in explaining his statement 'and for a poor pilgrim' that the poor person is one for whom charity is permissible, and if he has real estate and a servant...
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the matn's statement 'ولطفله الفقير' (and for his poor child), which establishes the obligation of the father to provide maintenance (nafaqah), lodging (sakan), and clothing (kiswah) for his young, poor child. The proof is Quran 2:233, which is primarily about mothers but serves as an indication that children's maintenance is the father's responsibility. The commentary then defines the term 'child' (tifl) as a minor who cannot earn, and discusses at what age the obligation ends—it continues until the male reaches the age of earning (had al-kasb), even before puberty. The child must also be free; a slave child's maintenance is on the owner. The passage then explores various financial scenarios: when both father and child are wealthy, the father spends from his own wealth, but if the child's property is absent, the father spends and can seek recourse from the judge. If the child has property like real estate or clothes, the father can use them for maintenance. If both are poor, the father must earn or even beg, and if he refuses, he can be imprisoned. The father has the right to hire out his male child to cover maintenance, but not his female child. If the father is wasteful, his control is restricted. When the father is poor and the child is wealthy, the child's maintenance is not upon the father; rather, the child must support the father. The commentary also addresses special cases such as a child with a waqf entitlement and whether a mother can sell the child's house for maintenance. The discussion ends with a reference to the definition of 'poor' (faqir).

Key Terms
الطفل (aṭ-ṭifl): A minor child who is incapable of earning; includes male and female from birth until the age of earning or puberty.
النفقة (an-nafaqah): Maintenance including food, lodging, and clothing, obligatory on the father for his poor minor child.
الفقير (al-faqīr): One who has no wealth or property sufficient to cover maintenance; the child must be poor for the father's obligation to apply.
الكسب (al-kasb): Earning; the ability to earn determines when the father's obligation ceases for a male child.
المبذر (al-mubadhdhir): A wasteful person; if the father is wasteful, the child's earnings are placed with a trustee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, until what age can a father hire out his male child for work to cover his maintenance?
A) Until he reaches puberty.
B) Until he reaches the age of earning (had al-kasb).
C) Until he reaches 15.
D) Until he becomes financially independent.

Key Takeaway
A father must provide maintenance for his poor minor child until the child can earn; if the child has wealth, the obligation shifts to that wealth.

Maintenance When Father and Child Are Both Rich
Definition and ruling for rich father and child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father must provide maintenance for his minor child and identify when he can legally reclaim expenses.

Core Idea
A father must support his minor child even if both are rich, but reclaiming expenses requires judicial permission—without it, the right is only ethical, not legal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a subtle yet critical point in Hanafi family law: the father's duty to maintain his minor child when both have wealth. While the obligation is clear, the right to recover spent funds hinges on formal judicial authorization. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying fiqh accurately.

Guided Path
1
Define the term 'tifl' and its scope
Identify that 'tifl' applies to both male and female minors, and that maintenance is not obligatory for adults except under special conditions.
The passage confirms gender-inclusive language for minor children.
2
State the general conditions for nafaqah obligation
List the conditions: the child must be poor, free, and minor. If the child has wealth, maintenance is from that wealth, not the father.
Restriction to freedom is also necessary—a slave child's maintenance is on the master.
3
Analyze the scenario when both father and child are rich
Even if both are rich, the father must spend from his own wealth if the child's wealth is absent (e.g., in another location).
This obligation is immediate and not contingent on the child's wealth being available.
4
Understand the reclaim rules
To reclaim the spent amount in court, the father must either have obtained prior judicial permission or have borne witness to his intention to reclaim. Without these, he has no legal right to reclaim.
Spending with the intention to reclaim is insufficient for court enforcement.
5
Distinguish between legal and ethical reclaim
Without judicial authorization, the father cannot reclaim in court, but ethically (between him and Allah) he may consider it permissible to reclaim.
This distinction highlights the interface between fiqh and qada (legal enforcement).

Source Text

Until he reaches puberty, and it is said: a young girl is called 'tifl' and 'tifla' as in al-Mughrib, and by this it is known that the term tifl applies to male and female. Hence he expressed it this way because maintenance is not obligatory on the father for the adult except with conditions we will mention. He restricted it to the poor because if a minor has wealth, his maintenance is from his wealth. And the restriction to freedom is necessary because, as we mentioned earlier, if a slave child, his maintenance is on his master, not on his father, whether the father is free or a slave. If he spends without his order, he has no right to reclaim in the court unless he bore witness that he spent intending to reclaim. If he did not bear witness but spent with the intention of reclaiming, he has no right to reclaim in the court, but between him and Allah Almighty, reclaiming is permissible for him.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the term 'tifl' (child) and establishes the basic conditions for the father's obligation to provide maintenance (nafaqah). The author clarifies that the obligation applies to minor children who are both poor and free. If the child has wealth, maintenance is taken from that wealth. The passage then discusses the scenario where both father and child are wealthy. In that case, the father must support the child from his own pockets if the child's wealth is present. If the child's wealth is absent (e.g., in another location), the father is still obligated to spend but may only reclaim the amount if he obtains prior permission from a judge. Spending without judicial permission, even with the intention of reclaiming, is valid in the eyes of Allah but not actionable in court. This distinction between judicial enforcement and ethical permissibility is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
طفل (tifl): A minor child who has not reached puberty, applying to both male and female.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance covering food, clothing, and shelter owed by a father to his minor child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, reclaiming maintenance spent on a minor child requires prior judicial permission to be enforceable in court.

Selling Child's Property and Hiring Out Male Children
Rulings when child has property, both are poor, father rich child poor, and hiring out male children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on maintenance of minor children in four scenarios: child owns property, both poor, father rich child poor, father poor child rich, and identify the rules regarding hiring out male and female children.

Core Idea
Maintenance of minor is wajib; child's property may be sold; male child may be hired out, but female never.

In this lesson, we examine key rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the maintenance of minor children. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance depending on the financial circumstances of the father and child, including the permissibility of selling the child's property and hiring out a male child to cover expenses. We will also clarify a common misunderstanding about hiring out daughters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the financial scenario
Determine whether the child owns property, whether the father is rich or poor, and whether the child is male or female. This step sets the foundation for applying the correct ruling.
2
Apply the corresponding ruling
For each scenario, state the obligation of maintenance and what actions are permitted (selling property, hiring out, etc.). Use the passage as reference.
3
Note the gender distinction
Recognize that hiring out applies only to male children after earning age; females are never hired out for maintenance. This is a key distinction.
4
Consider the spendthrift father exception
Understand that if the father is a spendthrift, the child's earnings are handed to a trustee, similar to other properties.
5
Reflect on juristic expansions
Note additional issues such as whether a child's share in waqf counts as absent property and whether a mother can sell the child's house for maintenance.

وإن كان للصغير عقاراً وأردية أو ثياب واحتيج إلى النفقة كان للأب أن يبيع ذلك كله وينفق عليه لأنه غني بهذه الأشياء. وإن كانا فقيرين فعند الخصاف أن يبيع الأب بتكفف الناس وينفق على أولاده الصغار، وقيل نفقتهم في بيت المال. هذا إذا كان عاجزاً عن الكسب وإن كان قادراً على الكسب اكتسب وأنفق فإن امتنع عن الكسب حبس بخلاف سائر الديون. ولا يحبس والد إن علا في دين ولده وإن سفل إلا في النفقة لأن في الامتناع عن الإنفاق إتلاف النفس، وإذا لم يف كسبه بحاجته أو لم يكتسب لعدم تيسره أنفق عليهم القريب ورجع على الأب إذا أيسر وإن كان الأب غنياً والولد الصغير فقيراً فالنفقة على الأب إلى أن يبلغ الذكر حد الكسب وإن لم يبلغ الحلم، فإذا كان هذا كان للأب أن يؤاجره وينفق عليه من أجرته وليس له في الأنثى ذلك، فلو كان الأب مبذراً يدفع كسب الابن إلى أمين كما في سائر أملاكه، وإن كان الأب فقيراً والصغير غنياً لا تجب نفقته على أبيه بل نفقة أبيه عليه ؛ كذا في الذخيرة وذكر الولوالجي أن في كل موضع أوجبنا نفقة الولد فإنه يدخل ويافع ومرهق وبالغ. وما قاله بعضهم هو المعروف الآن في بلادنا والمشهور فيما بينهم فعليه تحصل غاية المناسبة في الشرح أن يقال أراد بالطفل العاجز عن الكسب الخ فتأمل قوله : (وإن كان مال الصغير غائباً الخ) أقول: وقد سئلت عن صبي لا مال له غير أن له استحقاقاً في غلة وقف هل يكون بمنزلة ماله الغائب أو يكون بمنزلة من لا مال له أصلا؟ ولم أر من صرح بالمسألة والظاهر الأول حتى إذا أنفق بإذن القاضي له الرجوع فليتأمل رملي قوله : ( وإن كان له عقار الخ) أقول : ومثل الأب في ذلك الأم وهي واقعة الفتوى إذا أمر القاضي أمهم بالإنفاق عليهم وليس لهم سوى حصة من دار يسكنونها هل تباع في نفقتهم أم لا؟ والذي يظهر أنها تباع في ذلك وتنفق عليهم من ثمنها والسكنى من النفقة وإذا فرغ وجبت عليها رملي أقول : الظاهر أن مراد المؤلف بقوله «وإن كان له عقار الخ» إذا كان الصغير لا يحتاج إلى ذلك، أما إذا كان محتاجاً لسكنى عقاره ولبس ثيابه وأرديته لا فائدة في بيع ذلك لأنه لو باعها يحتاج إلى شراء غيرها وانظر ما سيذكره المؤلف عن البدائع في شرح قوله ولفقير محرم من أن الفقير من تحل له الصدقة وأنه لو كان له عقار وخادم

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on maintenance of minor children by examining various scenarios. First, if the child owns property (real estate, clothing), the father may sell it to cover maintenance. Second, if both father and child are poor, the father should beg or, if able, earn; if he refuses, he can be imprisoned—a unique severity not applied to other debts. Third, if the father is rich and child poor, maintenance is obligatory until the male child can earn (typically around puberty), and the father may hire out the male child to cover costs; this does not apply to female children. Fourth, if the father is poor and child rich, the child's wealth is used first, and the child even owes maintenance to the father. The passage also includes juristic commentary on whether a child's share in waqf income counts as 'absent property' and whether a mother can sell the child's house for maintenance. The distinction between male and female regarding hiring out is emphasized: males can be hired out to learn a trade, but females cannot be hired out due to modesty concerns.

Key Terms
يؤاجر (yu'ajjir): To hire out (the male child) for work or service to cover maintenance expenses.
تكفف (takaffuf): Begging, as a means for the poor father to obtain maintenance for his children.
حبس (habs): Imprisonment, the penalty for a father who refuses to earn or sell property for child maintenance.

Mother's Custody, Breastfeeding, and Maintenance in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on mother's right to maintenance, breastfeeding, and conflict resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a mother is legally compelled to breastfeed, distinguish it from religious duty, and explain the rules for allocating child maintenance to the mother.

Core Idea
A mother is not legally compelled to breastfeed unless the child’s life depends on it—then compulsion overrides reluctance.

This lesson unpacks three interconnected rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: (1) the mother as the preferred recipient of child maintenance and how disputes are resolved, (2) the critical distinction between religious and legal obligation to breastfeed, and (3) the effect of a child's earning capacity on maintenance. Understanding these distinctions is essential for applying Hanafi family law correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mother's role in receiving maintenance
The passage establishes that the mother is the preferred person to receive child maintenance from the father. If the father accuses her of misuse, his claim is not accepted without proof, and the judge may investigate discreetly through neighbors. The judge may also appoint a third party to disburse the maintenance in installments.
This reflects the principle that the mother is a trustee (amin) over the maintenance.
2
Distinguish between religious duty and legal compulsion for breastfeeding
The passage begins by stating the mother is not compelled by court (qada) to breastfeed, but she is instructed religiously (diyana). However, it then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that she is compelled if the father has no money and the child cannot take other food, because withholding would cause death.
Key terms: 'la tujbaru qada'an' vs 'tu'maru diyanatan'.
3
Analyze the effect of a child's earning capacity on maintenance
The passage clarifies that if a male child can earn (e.g., through work), his maintenance is no longer obligatory on the father, because the need is repelled. For a female child, however, maintenance remains obligatory even if she earns, because her earnings are not used for her own support (she is not hired out).
This rule applies to the father; other relatives are even more exempt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وتضيق عليهم لا يقبل قوله لأنها أمينة ودعوى الخيانة على الأمين لا تسمع من غير حجة : فإن قال للقاضي سل جيرانها فالقاضي يسأل جيرانها احتياطاً وإنما من كان يداخلها، فإن أخبر جيرانها بمال قال الأب زجرها ولده الصغير من نفسه وشرائه كذلك. وقال بعضهم : لأن العاقد الأب من جانب نفسه والأم من جانب الصغار لأن نفقتهم من أسباب التربية والحضانة وهي للأم . ثم ينظر إن كان ما وقع عليه الصلح أكثر من نفقتهم بزيادة يسيرة فهو عفو وهي ما تدخل تحت تقدير المقدرين وإن كان لا تدخل طرحت عنه، وإن كان المصالح عليه أقل بأن كان لا يكفيهم يزاد إلى مقدار كفايتهم. قوله : (ولا تجبر أمه لترضع لأنه كالنفقة وهي على الأب وعسى لا تقدر فلا تجبر عليه قضاء وتؤمر به ديانة لأنه من باب الاستخدام وهو واجب عليها ديانة كما قدمناه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الأب لا يجد من يرضعه أو كان الولد لا يأخذ ثدي غيرها. ونقل الزيلعي والإتقاني أنه ظاهر الرواية لأنه يتغذى بالدهن وغيره من المائعات فلا يؤدي إلى ضياعه، ونقل عدم الإجبار في هذه الحالة في المجتبى عن البعض ثم قال والأصح أنها تجبر عند الكل اهـ . وجزم به في الهداية، وفي الخانية وعليه الفتوى، وذكر في فتح القدير أنه الأصوب لأن قصر الرضيع الذي لم يأنس الطعام على الدهن والشراب سبب تمريضه وموته اهـ. وفي الخانية : وإن لم يكن للأب ولا للولد الصغير مال تجبر الأم على الإرضاع عند الكل اهـ. فجعل يستحق النفقة، وانظر ما كتبناه هناك أيضاً يظهر لك الأمر قوله : (فإذا كان هذا) أي بلغ حد الكسب . قال في التتارخانية : ولو أراد الأب أن يؤاجرهم أي الذكور في عمل أو أخدمة فله ذلك لأن فيه منفعة للصغير لأنه يتعلم الكسب، أما قبل أن يتعلم أو بعده ولكن لا يحسن العمل فنفقته على الأب اهـ. قال الرملي : وصرح به أيضاً كثير من علمائنا :قال وبه علم أن غير الأب من المحارم لا تجب نفقة القادر على الكسب عليه من باب أولى لأنها لدفع الحاجة وقد اندفعت وصار غنياً بكسبه فلا حاجة إلى إيجابها على الفقير كما هو ظاهر وهي واقعة الفتوى وقد أفتيت فيها بعدم الوجوب اهـ قوله : (وليس له في الأنثى ذلك قال الرملي : لو استغنت بنحو خياطة وغزل يجب أن تكون نفقتها
He asks about his children and the children of daughters and sons. In al-Dhakhira: If the mother disputes regarding maintenance of the children, the judge imposes on the father maintenance for the poor minors and gives the maintenance to her because she is more compassionate to the children. If the father says she does not spend and is harsh on them, his statement is not accepted because she is a trustee, and a claim of betrayal against a trustee is not heard without proof. If he says to the judge: ask her neighbors, the judge asks her neighbors as a precaution, but only those who interact with her. If the neighbors inform that she has money, the father's statement, the judge restrains her and prevents her from that for their interest. Some of our scholars said: when conflict occurs between the spouses likewise and the amount of maintenance is known, the judge has the option: if he wishes, he gives it to a trustworthy person who gives it to her morning and evening, not as a lump sum; and if he wishes, he orders someone else to spend on the children. If the woman reconciles with her husband regarding maintenance of the minors, whether the husband is wealthy or poor, it is permissible. The scholars differed on the method of permissibility of this reconciliation: some said because the contractor is the father from both sides, like his sale of his minor child's property to himself and his purchase likewise. Some said because the contractor is the father from his side and the mother from the side of the minors, because their maintenance is part of upbringing and custody, and custody belongs to the mother. Then he looks: if what was agreed upon is more than their maintenance by a small increase, it is forgiven and does not fall under the estimation of estimators; if it does not fall, it is deducted; if what was agreed upon is less such that it does not suffice them, it is increased to the amount that suffices them. His saying: (And his mother is not compelled to breastfeed) because it is like maintenance, which is on the father, and perhaps she cannot, so she is not compelled by the court, but is instructed religiously because it falls under utilization, and it is incumbent upon her religiously as we mentioned. He made it absolute, so it includes when the father cannot find a wet nurse or when the child only takes her breast. Al-Zayla'i and al-Itqani transmitted that it is the apparent narration because the child is nourished by oil and other liquids, so it does not lead to his loss. He transmitted non-compulsion in this case in al-Mujtaba from some, then said: the correct view is that she is compelled according to all. This is affirmed in al-Hidayah, and in al-Khaniyya it is the fatwa. In Fath al-Qadir, he mentioned that it is the most correct because restricting an infant who has not become familiar with food to oil and drink causes his illness and death. In al-Khaniyya: if the father and the minor child have no money, the mother is compelled to breastfeed according to all. So he made it [the child] entitled to maintenance. And look at what we wrote there as well, the matter will become clear to you. His saying: (When this happens) meaning he has reached the age of earning. He said in al-TatarKhaniyya: If the father wants to hire out them, i.e., the males, for work or service, he may do so because it benefits the minor as he learns earning. But before he learns or after but does not do the work well, his maintenance is on the father. Al-Ramli said: Many of our scholars also stated this. He said: By this it is known that other than the father of the relatives, maintenance of the one able to earn is not obligatory on him a fortiori because it is to repel need, and the need is repelled and he has become rich by his earning, so there is no need to oblige it on the poor, as is apparent, and it is a fatwa case, and I have given fatwa of non-obligation. His saying: (And he does not have that for the female). Al-Ramli said: If she becomes independent through embroidery and spinning, her maintenance must be...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers several important rulings related to maintenance of children. First, the mother's role: she is the preferred recipient of child maintenance from the father because she is kinder. The father's accusation of misuse is not accepted without proof; neighbors may be asked. The judge may appoint a third party to dispense maintenance or give it in installments. Reconciliation between parents on maintenance amount is valid, with scholars differing on its legal construction. Second, the breastfeeding ruling: the mother is not compelled by court to breastfeed, as it is considered maintenance which is the father's duty. However, she is religiously encouraged to do so. If the child cannot take food and only survives on breastmilk, or if the father cannot find a wet nurse, then the mother is compelled to breastfeed according to the correct view (mufta bihi), as withholding would cause the child's death. This is the relied-upon position. Third, the passage reiterates that a male child who can earn does not obligate others to support him; a female child, however, must be supported even if she earns, because her earnings are not used for her maintenance (due to not being hired out). The commentary by al-Ramli clarifies that if a female is self-sufficient through sewing, her maintenance is still obligatory on the father.

Key Terms
إرضاع (irda'): Breastfeeding of the infant; it is a duty upon the mother religiously but not legally enforceable except when necessary.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter; obligatory on the father for his minor children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (mufta bihi), when is a mother legally compelled to breastfeed?
A) Only if the father asks and pays her.
B) Only if the mother has no money and the child is hungry.
C) When the father and child have no money and the child cannot eat other food.
D) Always if the child is under 2 years old.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a mother's breastfeeding is a religious duty but is only legally enforceable when the child's survival depends on it and alternative means are absent.
